ALBANIA
IRRIGATION REHABILITATION PROJECT
CREDIT AND PROJECT SUMMARY
Borrower:         Government of Albania
Executing         Ministry of Agriculture and Food, selected Water Enterprises,
Agencies:         Soil Institute, Institute for Study and Design of Water Works
Beneficiaries:    About 56,800 farm families in seven districts along the coastal plains who will
benefit from increased and more reliable supply of irrigation water and improved
drainage. Local private contractors and construction material suppliers.
Credit Amount:   SDR7. 1 million (US$10.0 million equivalent)
Terms:            Forty years maturity with a ten year grace period. Credit proceeds will be lent to
the Government at the standard IDA service charge.
Project           (a) to increase agricultural production by raising farm productivity through rapid
Objectives:       rehabilitation of the existing irrigation and drainage infrastructure;
(b) to alleviate poverty by increasing income of small private farmers and creating
employment opportunities for dependent farm labor in rural areas;
(c) to ensure the long-term sustainability of irrigation and drainage investments
through farmer participation in operation and maintenance, efficient system
management and adequate cost recovery.
Project           (a) System Rehabilitation: The project would rehabilitate 21 irrigation schemes
Description:      serving a command area of 73,500 ha, at a per-ha cost of US$470 equivalent, and
18 drainage schemes covering 99,300 ha at a unit cost of US$190 per ha.
Rehabilitation works would include earthworks and canal cleaning, canal lining,
replacement of pumping equipment, repair of control structures and gates, and
installation of flow measuring devices. A demonstration scheme for canal flow
control, covering a command area of 1,000 ha, would be built for training of
Albanian irrigation system designers and operators.
(b) Institutional Development: Technical assistance, comprehensive training
programs and related office, laboratory and maintenance equipment would help
improve system operation and maintenance, enhance the local engineering design
capacity, establish Water User Associations in the project area, and implement an
On-Farm Water Management program
(c) Policy Support: Studies would be undertaken to finalize the regulatory
framework for irrigation and drainage, to prepare the turnover of small systems
to water users, to consolidate initially small Water User Associations into larger
water user boards, to develop a financial model for determining cost-covering
water charges, and to promote private surface pumping and groundwater
development.



- 11 -
(d) Implementation Support: Technical assistance, training and office equipment
would be provided for a Project Management Unit in the Ministry of Agriculture
and Food.
Environmental    The project has been placed in environmental screening category "B" consistent
Aspects:          with provisions of Operational Directive 4.01, 'Environmental Assessment'. The
project would have a positive environmental impact through a reduction of surface
water and soil contamination stemming from agricultural runoff by reducing water
losses through improved system conveyance and flow control. Pesticide runoff
would be further reduced through dissemination of information to farmers on the
safe use of pesticides, following Bank regulations on techniques for integrated pest
management. Rehabilitation of major drainage systems along coastal areas would
improve flood control and reduce salinity-related problems caused by water-
logging. Schemes included for rehabilitation under the project have undergone an
environmental screening during project preparation; an additional comprehensive
environmental evaluation would be required for each scheme prior to inviting bids
for civil works contracts.
Benefits:         The project would cause an increase in crop yields and a shift in cropping patterns
towards high-value cash crops, resulting in a significant increase of physical
production and output value, mainly for wheat, maize, vegetables, milk, fodder,
tobacco and cotton. Most incremental production would initially be for self-
consumption until a rise in domestic purchasing power and improved export
prospects would enhance prospects for marketing of surplus produce. Annual net
cash income of benefitting farmers, after on-farm consumption, would exceed
US$500 by year five of the project, compared to US$200 without the project. The
project would provide the basis for a sound water pricing policy aiming, over a
five-year period, at the full recovery of operation and maintenance costs borne by
Water Enterprises, and the recovery of all investment and replacement costs
currently borne by Government, through cost-covering water charges payable by
water users. Strengthening the role of women in irrigation management through
their active involvement in Water User Associations would be a focus of training
activities under the project.
Risks:            The main project risks stem from the limited implementation capacity of the
various Government institutions involved, potentially causing delays in design,
procurement and construction of rehabilitation works.  The comprehensive
technical assistance and training program under the project would help reduce
these risks. There are also risks of delays in securing all required cofinancing.
However, given the fully modular design of the project, with independent
rehabilitation works to be carried out in seven different districts, such delays
would have no negative impact on the economic rate of return, since all required
institutional development and implementation support would be financed by IDA.
The potential lack of funds to cover local costs of 37 percent of total project costs
is a further risk but would be limited since 80 percent of local costs would be
financed through participation of IDA and other cofinanciers. During the mid-
term review, a broad assessment of project implementation and financing would
be undertaken with the view of addressing and resolving any remaining risks.

ALBANIA
IRRIGATION REHABILITATION PROJECT
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
CHAPTER I. THE IRRIGATION SECTOR
A. Introduction
1.1   Need for Irrigation. Albania is commonly divided into three agro-ecological zones based on
climate, soils and vegetation -- the fertile coastal plains, the intermediate hilly region, and the non-arable
mountain zones. Coastal areas are characterized by a Mediterranean climate with dry, hot summers and
mild rainy winters. Annual rainfall varies from 800-2,000 mm in coastal areas, to about 1,600-2,000 mm
in the hilly region, and up to 3,000 mm in the mountains. Less than 20 percent of annual rainfall occurs
in the six-month period April-September. Crop water deficits between June and August range between 400
and 500 mm which cannot be supplied from soil moisture, making irrigation necessary for adequate crop
growth, especially in the coastal areas. Alluvial soils in the coastal plains are predominantly planted with
cereals, forage, vegetables, and industrial crops. Agriculture in the hills is more diverse, including
deciduous fruits, grapes and vineyards, olives and citrus to the south, potatoes and industrial crops, and
sugar beet in the eastern upland plateau. Albania has a total land area of about 2.9 ha million, of which
702,000 ha are arable land, 409,000 ha million are pastures, and 1.05 ha million are forests. Some
417,000 ha are under the command of irrigation systems, covering about 60 percent of the national arable
area and accounting for some 80 percent of agricultural production value. More than half of the irrigation
command area, or 226,000 ha, is located in the coastal plains.
1.2   Historic Developments. Irrigation has been practiced in Albania since the beginning of this
century. In 1938, the irrigated area covered 29,000 ha or 10 percent of arable land. Massive investments
under the communist regime, most of them carried out between 1950 and 1975, increased the area under
irrigation command to the present area. The development of irrigation was accompanied by flood
protection works for 130,000 ha and drainage systems for 300,000 ha. Furthermore, extensive reclamation
has been carried out since the mid-1930s to reclaim 280,000 ha of marshlands and saline areas along the
coast now forming two thirds of the irrigation command area.
1.3   Present Status.  Social upheavals following the demise of communism in 1991 resulted in
substantial damage to the irrigation and drainage systems, mainly affecting pumping stations, transformer
stations, high-tension lines, hydraulic structures and concrete lining of irrigation canals. Most of the
damage occurred on structures serving former agricultural cooperatives, accounting for 74 percent of the
irrigation command area, while systems on former state farms remained generally unharmed. At the same
time, regular system maintenance has been reduced considerably and new construction work has been
stopped due to central budget constraints. As a result of these developments, irrigation systems on 114,000
ha are currently inoperative, and drainage systems on some 153,000 ha are severely damaged. In addition,
the process of dismantling cooperatives, liquidating state farms and privatizing all arable land created the
major challenge of delivering water to a multitude of small farmers and collecting water charges from
them, a task for which the existing institutions are not prepared. Thus, only some 80,000 ha were actually
irrigated in 1993 (130,000 ha in 1992), accounting for a mere 19 percent of the irrigation command area
(31 percent in 1992), or 11 percent of arable land (18 percent in 1992). This is a dramatic development
in light of the urgent need for a rise in farm productivity in order to ensure the viability of the new
smallholder agriculture in the country.



- 2 -
B. Irrigated Agriculture
1.4    The Agriculture Sector.  Albania is predominantly an agricultural country.  At present, the
agriculture sector generates about half of GDP and is the main source of employment for 50 percent of the
labor force (705,000 persons in primary production and an additional 100,000 in related sectors). Two-
thirds of the national population live in rural areas. When compared with its ex-socialist neighbors,
Albania has achieved extraordinary progress towards reforming the agriculture sector along market
economy lines since the end of communism in 1991. About 90 percent of the land previously controlled
by state-run cooperatives and state farms has been fully privatized, major reforms in price and trade
liberalization have been achieved, and further reforms aiming at the privatization of agricultural enterprises,
the reform of the agricultural credit system, and the legal framework for land leasing are under way. After
a 40 percent aggregate decline in production during 1990 and 1991, the sector achieved a 17 percent
growth in production during 1992. Growth for 1993 is estimated at 12 percent, with a further expected
rise of 10 percent in 1994. After the emergence of some 400,000 family farms with an average size of
about 1.4 ha, cropping patterns have seen a shift away from wheat towards maize (as animal feed for the
increasing livestock herd) and high-value cash crops such as fruit and vegetables. Cropping intensities and
yields in the private farm sector are likely to rise during the coming years as the present subsistence
agriculture will become less important while the share of marketed agricultural produce will increase.
Rehabilitation of the irrigation and drainage infrastructure is a prerequisite for such a development.
1.5    Yield Response to Irrigation. Due to plentiful rainfall during autumn and winter, winter crops
grown in lowland plains such as wheat and fodder require only supplemental irrigation to compensate for
irregular spring rainfall. However, systematic water deficits in the lowland plains between April and
September prevent cultivation of summer crops such as alfalfa, maize, fruits and vegetables, and industrial
crops (i.e. cotton) unless full irrigation is provided.
Average yield response to irrigation in Albania is

1.7    Water Resources. Albania is a water-rich country with an annual average of 3,080 m3 per capita,
the highest value in Central and Eastern Europe. Total run-off equals on average 25.7 billion m3 per year,
of which only 2 percent or 558 million m3 can be stored in irrigation reservoirs. This water, together with



- 3 -
450 million m3 diverted from rivers and lakes, constitutes the present volume of 1.0 billion m3 available
for irrigation from surface water. To augment water supply further, 14 reservoirs are under construction
(discontinued due to lack of funds), and 9 reservoirs have been designed for future implementation.
Groundwater resources have played no significant role in irrigation.
C. Irrigation and Drainage Infrastructure
1.8    System Size and Fragmentation. Irrigation systems are highly fragmented since 55 percent of
the irrigation command area is supplied by small systems covering less than 5,000 ha. Some 653 dams
and reservoirs supply water to about 184,000 ha, and 639 pumping stations with some 1,250 electrical
pumps and an installed power of about 200,000 kW provide lift irrigation from rivers and lakes for about
78,000 ha. Run-of-river schemes account for water on some 160,000 ha, with groundwater supplying the
remaining 1,000 ha of land under irrigation command. Reservoirs and run-of-river schemes are generally
in working order while more than half of installed irrigation pumps need replacement. Very small systems
in the hills and mountains, serving less than 200 ha of land and involving high pumping of more than 50m,
are common and mostly non-viable from  an economic point of view.' Lift irrigation in coastal areas
implies generally less than 20m of lift. Annex 2.1 provides detailed information on the existing irrigation
and drainage infrastructure in each district.
1.9    System  Layout.  Irrigation schemes have been designed to deliver water to rather large areas
belonging to former agricultural cooperatives and state farms. Thus, the existing irrigation grid provides
for hydraulic units of about 100 ha served by secondary canals and sub-units of 10 ha served by tertiary
canals (see Annex 2.2). In most instances, this system layout allowed for a smooth conversion to private
irrigated plots of about I ha (200mx5Om), served by quaternary field ditches departing perpendicular from
tertiary canals, without loss of irrigation efficiency. Complete loss of access to irrigation water following
land distribution could generally be avoided.
1.10   Delivery Capacities. Irrigation is supplemental to rainfall and varies between 900 and 7,000 m3/ha
at the water source depending upon location and crop. Schemes constructed after 1985 are designed to
supply the full crop water deficits. However, this applies to only 8 percent or 35,000 ha of the national
command area. All other schemes have design capacities of 2,800 m3/ha (plains) and 2,500 m3/ha (hills)
at the crop.  Losses were added at 30 percent to design the conveyance system  capacity of 0.5
liter/second/ha. However, given actual losses of 70 percent, only an inadequate average amount of about
1,200 m3/ha actually reaches the plant, far below full crop water requirements at peak demand periods,
thus reducing yields and irrigation intensities. High losses are mainly due to missing or looted canal lining
and the low efficiency of irrigation methods (para 1.11). Simple measures to rehabilitate these schemes
and improve conveyance systems would reduce losses and result in an average increase of available water
by 33 percent or 400 m3/ha to reach a total of 1,600 m3/ha supplied to the crop.
1.11   Irrigation Methods. Except for sprinkler systems on large plots owned by joint-ventures with
foreign partners, surface irrigation methods dominate. Furrow-type and strip-type systems account for
about 95 percent of the methods used. Grading of furrows is generally inadequate, and water use
/f    Out of 639 pumping stations, only 10 have capacities above 1,000 1/s serving a total of some 22,800 ha of irrigated land.
The remaining 629 stations provide water to an area of 55,200 ha, equivalent to an average of a mere 88 ha per station and
irrigation system. Equally, out of 653 reservoirs, the 14 largest ones account for one third of the total live storage capacity
of all reservoirs, with the remaining 639 smaller reservoirs serving an average irrigated area of only 200 ha per reservoir and
system.



- 4 -
efficiencies do not exceed 60 percent and are even lower for strip irrigation. Fish-bone systems irrigate
5 percent of land under surface irrigation and prove to be more efficient and well suited to the needs of
smallholder farmers.
1.12   Drainage Systems. Drainage covers only areas that are also irrigated during the dry season. Of
the 276,000 ha artificially drained, about 206,000 ha are drained by gravity and 70,000 ha by electrical
pumping stations. About 200,000 have open drains throughout and 76,000 ha are partly drained by
subsurface tile drains. Total drainage canal length is 15,700 km. Existing drainage systems cover virtually
all land needing drainage. The 25 electrical pumping stations with an installed power of 22,700 kW were
a primary target of looting and destruction following the dismantling of agricultural cooperatives in 1991,
leaving only some 123,000 ha with functioning drains.
D. Organization of the Sector
1.13   Ministry of Agriculture and Food. Overall supervision of the irrigation sector is the responsibility
of the Land, Water and Rural Development Department (LWRD) in the Ministry of Agriculture and Food
(MoAF). LWRD consists of a director and ten employees which are dealing with all aspects of land
registration and cadastre, soil protection and improvement, irrigation and drainage, land reclamation, and
rural development. The three employees in the irrigation division are overseeing: (i) activities of 39,
generally district-based public Water Enterprises (WEs) responsible for system operation and maintenance;
(ii) all works carried out by the 21 public Construction Enterprises (CEs) specialized in irrigation, drainage
and flood control;2  (iii) engineering services provided by the Institute for Studies and Design of Water
Works (ISDWW); and (iv) studies and research on soil improvement conducted by the Soil Institute (SI).
MoAF is currently reorganizing the ministry's organizational structure, involving the creation of a new
Directorate of Services for Natural Water Resources. The Directorate will include four separate divisions
for water (including irrigation), land, fisheries and environment. Such action will certainly strengthen the
administrative capacity of MoAF in dealing with the irrigation sub-sector.
1.14   Water Enterprises. Prior to the dissolution of agricultural cooperatives and state farms, WEs were
in charge of: (i) distributing irrigation water up to the tertiary level; (ii) maintaining all primary and
secondary canals, including structures in tertiary canals; (iii) operating and maintaining most irrigation and
drainage pumping stations, reservoirs, roads, flood and riverbank protection works; (iv) collecting water
charges, and coordinating water requests and water distribution; and (v) entering into contracts with public
CEs for system rehabilitation and expansion. Water distribution and canal maintenance at the tertiary and
quaternary level were handled by cooperatives and state farms. Today, WEs face the major challenge of
delivering water to the multitude of individual farmers and collecting water charges from them, and the
responsibility for operation and maintenance of systems below the secondary level remains unclear. While
WEs have lost cooperatives and state farms as their contracting partner and major source of revenue,
financial support to WEs from the central budget has been reduced from US$18 per ha in 1990 to about
US$9 per ha in 1992 and is expected to equal US$5 per ha in 1993.3 Starting in 1992, most WEs have
responded by requiring private farmers to elect village representatives who ensure collection and payment
of water charges prior to water delivery through the WE.  Farmer participation in this system  is
2/     In addition to these 21 CEs, eight of the 39 WEs were combined WEs-CEs to carry out irrigation construction works in their
respective districts, resulting in a total of 29 CEs. Works in the remaining seven districts were covered through CEs in
neighboring districts.
3/     Until 1990, WEs recovered about 75 percent of their operating expenses through water charges paid by agricultural
cooperatives and state farms, the balance was covered through direct subsidies from the MoAF budget.



- 5 -
encouraging. Nevertheless, insufficient funding and lack of maintenance has resulted in a reduction of WE
staff and the depletion of most equipment and buildings.4
1.15   Construction Enterprises.  These enterprises had a monopoly for all works in irrigation and
drainage. Works were financed by WEs through MoAF contributions, and any losses were covered
through direct subsidies from MoAF. Since January of 1993, CE are considered "financially autonomous"
and receive no more budgetary support from MoAF. At the same time, CEs remain public enterprises
without management autonomy and, therefore, cannot compete with emerging private contractors which,
in turn, are free to engage in various construction activities, pay competitive salaries and invest in new
machinery. Since all on-going irrigation construction works funded by MoAF have been halted, CEs are
left with no source of income and practically stopped operating. Staff has mostly been laid off, equipment
and buildings are beyond their useful life.5
1.16   Legal and Regulatory Framework. Distribution of water for irrigation is still being carried out
based on laws regulating the relation between the state as the owner of water resources, WEs as distributors
and system operators, and cooperatives and state farms as water users.6 With the dissolution of the latter
water users, this legal framework has become obsolete, leaving a vacuum with respect to the legal basis
for sale of water by WEs and the settlement of disputes between private irrigators and WEs. Furthermore,
supervision of WE operations through MoAF is being carried out without sufficient staff and financial
resources and under no clear regulatory framework. This creates a non-sustainable situation where WEs
are forced to charge for irrigation water at below-cost levels, set centrally by MoAF, while not receiving
sufficient financial support from MoAF to cover operating losses.
E. Technical Options
1.17   Investment Alternatives. Capital investments into Albania's physical infrastructure for irrigation
and drainage require a choice among one or several of the following technical options:
(a) System Rehabilitation. Lack of maintenance and substantial damages to the infrastructure during
1991 have rendered inoperative irrigation systems covering 114,000 ha of the national command
area and drainage systems covering 153,000 ha of previously drained land (para 1.3).
Reestablishment of as-designed conditions through rehabilitation works would consist primarily in
replacing irrigation and drainage pumps and in repairing hydraulic structures such as measuring
devices, gates, lined canals, aqueducts, drainage service roads, and dams and reservoirs. This
would ensure water supply to now underirrigated and non-irrigated crops and would provide better
evacuation of drainage water and lower water tables.
4/      Currently, WEs employ a total of 5,500 employees, equivalent to an average of 1.3 workers per 100 ha of command area.
This is in line with international standards of staff requirements (between I and 2 workers per 100 ha) for operation and
maintenance of small irrigation systems with an average size of less than 300 ha per system.
5!      The total number of staff in CEs sank from 9,900 at end-1990 to 3,000 at end-1992 and less than 1,000 during the third
quarter of 1993.
6/      The legal basis for the activities of WEs are: (i) Decree No. 4311 of September 8, 1967, and (ii) Decision of the Council
of Ministers No. 130 of April 28, 1988, both dealing with "Construction and Administration of Irrigation and Drainage
Systems".



- 6 -
(b) Improvement of Conveyance Systens. Except for critical reaches, irrigation canals are unlined.
New lining of additional irrigation canals with concrete may save up to 80 percent of water lost
during its conveyance in unlined canals to farm gates. Canal lining would also increase carrying
capacities and allow additional water to be delivered provided water would be available at the
source, requiring additional investments for enlargement of reservoirs, expansion of run-of-river
inlet structures, and increasing pumping station capacities.
(c) Modernization of On-farm Distribution Systems.  The high proportion of irrigation with low
distribution efficiency under furrow-type and stripe-type systems presents an opportunity for
considerable upgrading of on-farm distribution systems (para 1.  11). Options include improvements
of furrow systems involving only modest investments into land grading to a constant slope and
head-end pipe-works. Investments into sprinkler, drip and spray types of distribution systems
would be capital-intensive, imply substantial energy cost and would not necessarily distribute water
much more efficiently than well operated surface systems in flat terrain such as in Albania's coastal
plains.
(d) Private Surface Pumping Development.  Numerous streams and lakes provide opportunities to
private farmers to irrigate presently non-irrigated lands in the vicinity of such water resources.
Small diesel powered pumps involving moderate capital investment and maintenance cost would be
able to irrigate up to 10 ha. Such equipment could also pump from drains within existing irrigation
systems, thus augmenting water supply for surface irrigation, provided drainage water supply is
dependable.
(e) Private Groundwater Development. Irrigation development with medium sized tubewells (3 to 5
I/s) has been ignored in the past due to the large size of cooperative and state farms. These
tubewells could be economic for farmers located on non-irrigated lands and land needing drainage
at present. However, small tubewell development (less than 1 l/s) suited for smallholder would
increase capital investments considerably with miniaturizing of technology.
(f) Completion of Unfinished Schemes. On-going works on some 34 major irrigation schemes and two
drainage schemes, benefitting a combined area of 100,000 ha of arable land, have been halted since
1991 due to lack of funds. On average, at 1991 prices, 40 percent of the initially projected total
cost has been invested into these projects. After comprehensive technical appraisal and economic
revaluation, some of these schemes might become attractive candidates for completion.
(g) Construction of New Schemes. Any expansion of the existing irrigation and drainage infrastructure
through new pumping schemes, run-of-river schemes or additional dams and reservoirs would
involve very high capital investments. Construction of new schemes for water augmentation, i.e.
through upgrading of system capacity in pumping stations and reservoirs, would require less capital
but would need to be combined with investments into improved conveyance.
1.18   Ranking of Options. As shown in table 1.2, estimated benefit/cost ratios for different farm models
indicate that general system rehabilitation through re-establishment of as-designed conditions is by far the
economically most attractive option for investments in the irrigation and drainage sector. Improvement of
conveyance systems and development of low-lift private surface pumping are the next-best investment
alternatives. Modernization of on-farm distribution systems through sprinklers could be considered in hilly
terrains with high concentration of citrus plantation. Groundwater development through deep tubewells



- 7 -
and construction of new schemes result in the lowest benefit/cost ratios and are not justified before other
investment alternatives have been implemented.

F. Institutional Issues
1.19   Organization of Water Users. Delivery of irrigation water to some 400,000 private farmers with
average holdings of 1.4 ha per farm is impossible without active farmer participation in water scheduling,
collection of water charges, and system operation and maintenance. A first and promising step towards
the formation of user groups is the recent emergence of elected village representatives who consolidate
water requests from individual farmers, collect water fees and enter into water contracts with WEs.
Commune-based irrigation technicians, employed by WEs and responsible for hydraulic units of up to
1,000 ha, deliver water to the tertiary canal level from where village representatives supervise the use of
water by individual farmers. Although unauthorized taking of water does not appear to be a major
problem, the mechanisms for control of water distribution and use are nevertheless clearly inadequate, often
resulting in a non-equitable distribution of water.  Furthermore, cleaning and repair of tertiary and
quaternary irrigation and drainage canals remains without organization. The establishment of Water User
Associations (WUAs) is, therefore, a pre-requisite to allow for orderly water distribution and to guarantee
system maintenance and safeguarding below the secondary level.
1.20   Basis for WUAs.  WUAs should ideally be based on hydraulic units, that is, areas between
secondary canals comprising irrigated land served by a single source, thus allowing for precise and direct



- 8 -
control of water amounts delivered to each WUA. Albania's irrigation system, however, has been designed
to accommodate former cooperatives and state farms, irrespective of long-established village boundaries,
and the command area between secondary canals generally concerns various villages. Establishment of
WUAs based on hydraulic units would, therefore, require farmers of different villages to cooperate in the
same user group. Since villages are currently the only well functioning local social unit, with sometimes
significant rivalry and conflicts between farmers living in different villages, it would be unrealistic to
initiate the process of forming WUAs on generally non-village-based units.  In addition, the recent
distribution of land has left some farmers with up to three fragmented plots, often scattered over a number
of independent hydraulic sub-systems. Farmers would thus be forced to become member in more than one
user group if WUAs were to be based on hydraulic units. This further supports the case for initially
village-based WUAs. After several years of successful operation, WUAs should eventually be grouped
together into larger organizations such as to comprise irrigated areas that are more closely based on
hydraulic units. These organizations would then take over operation and maintenance of secondary canals
within their command area.
1.21   Transfer of Small Systems. Albania's irrigation infrastructure is characterized by a very large
number of small individual systems, served by isolated low-capacity pumping stations or small reservoirs,
and covering a total of about 78,000 ha.  Operation and maintenance of these systems, previously
undertaken by former agricultural cooperatives, involves high per-ha costs, and many of the systems are
economically not justifiable. Decentralization of system management could reduce related costs through
more efficient local, private management and would allow to internalize costs at the user level. The
ownership and management responsibility for these small systems should, therefore, be transferred entirely
to local WUAs which would be required to cover all future costs of system operation and maintenance.
Farmers would then have a strong incentive to change their cropping patterns such as to either generate
sufficient cash income from higher-value crops in order to pay for system maintenance, or to switch to
crops requiring no irrigation and to abandon non-viable irrigation systems.'
1.22   Regulation and Consolidation of WEs. The 39 state-owned WEs need to be placed under a
regulatory framework based on the principles of management autonomy and public accountability.
Management autonomy involves the right of WEs to propose and revise water charges, subject to approval
by Government, based on each WE's actual operating cost, thereby enabling WEs to recover recurrent and
capital expenditures and become largely independent from budgetary transfers by MoAF. Granting of such
authority to WEs must go along with an active supervision of their activities through MoAF and a stronger
accountability towards water users and the general public. This includes annual independent audits of WE
accounts and their subsequent publication as a basis for justifying adjustments of water charges. Although
WEs would remain public sector enterprises, to improve their accountability further, the supervision of
their operations should, in the medium term, be transferred to private farmers at the district level through
representation of WUAs on the Board of Supervisors of WEs. In the long term, after WUAs have been
established and have taken over operation and maintenance of most canals, the present district-based
structure of WEs should be revised through consolidation of WEs into broader basin enterprises which
would be responsible for managing water use and water distribution in the six major river basins of the
country.
7/     As an interim measure and first step in this direction, a Presidential Decree (No. 576 of June 28, 1993) provides for the
turnover of management and ownership of small and independent irrigation systems, serving no more than one commune or
village, to the local commune administration. This concerns in particular areas where system management was carried out
by former agricultural cooperatives and state farms. The provisions of this Decree will need to be incorporated into a new
Irrigation Code.



- 9 -
1.23   Privatization of CEs and Promotion of Private Contractors. The 21 state-owned CEs are non-
operational and their assets are mostly depleted. Government intends to auction off CEs or to liquidate
them if no positive price can be achieved during the auction. In September 1993, MoAF handed over all
CEs to the National Agency for Privatization, and completion of privatization is expected by the summer
of 1994. Once privatized, these enterprises can bid for irrigation rehabilitation works on an equal footing
with the about 70 local private contractors and joint-ventures with foreign contractors that have emerged
in Albania since 1991. Small private contractors will have a major cost advantage over joint-ventures and
remaining privatized CEs due to lower overheads and greater adaptability of their enterprises. They should
be able to compete successfully for dispersed irrigation construction and repair works if contracts are kept
simple enough and are packaged at a scale suitable for small enterprises. As has happened during the past
two years, private contractors would absorb some of the labor released by privatized or liquidated former
CEs.
G. Financial Issues
1.24   System of Water Charges.  At present, water charges are centrally set by MoAF and are
differentiated according to potential land productivity. Current charge levels are Lek 1.8/mi3 in the plains,
Lek 1. 1/m3 in the hills, and Lek 0.5/m3 in mountain areas. However, in lack of measuring equipment on
most canals, WE convert the volume-based water charge into a surface-based fee, assuming a fixed average
discharge per irrigation application of generally 1,000 m3. This results in an equivalent charge of about
US$18 per ha and application in the plains. Farmers are charged separately and in advance for each
delivery. Albania must, therefore, be credited for having established a simple and effective charge system
with high collection rates that provides efficient water pricing on a per unit basis.
1.25   District-based Charges and Cost Recovery. While the principle of volume-based water charges
promotes efficient water use, the current charge levels are inadequate to ensure sufficient funding for
system operation and maintenance and for required investments into system rehabilitation. Furthermore,
water charges in hill and mountain areas are set at lower levels than in the plains while, in turn, operating
costs are higher in hills and mountains due to high pumping and extensive use of reservoirs for relatively
small irrigated areas. Finally, the extent of water lift and artificial drainage varies substantially between
schemes and regions, causing major cost differentials between districts; centrally-determined water fees,
however, do not allow for a regional differentiation of water charges based on actual costs. For these
reasons, the current level of water charges must be revised and differentiated on a regional basis, with the
aim of full recovery of recurrent and investment costs, by reflecting actual district-based expenditures
incurred by individual WEs.
1.26   Principles for Setting Water Charges. Upon proposal by each WE, district-based water charges
should be set by Government such as (i) to cover all justified costs of operation and maintenance of WEs,
based on independent external audits of their activities, and (ii) to recover the cost of investments
(rehabilitation and replacements) by Government into the infrastructure at the primary and secondary canal
level. Water charges would be payable on a volumetric basis by WUAs who would collect water charges
from their members. A portion of water charges would be payable prior to water delivery (e.g. 30
percent), the balance would be paid after the end of the cropping season. Except for the portion of charges
related to the recovery of investment costs, which would be transferred to the central Government budget,
revenues from water charges must be retained by WEs in order to preserve the direct financial link between
cost recovery and the actual use of funds for operating and maintaining systems at the primary and
secondary canal level. To cover costs of operation and maintenance of systems below the secondary level,
WUAs would set and collect membership fees.



- 10 -
H. Legal Issues
1.27  Legal Framework for Irrigation and Drainage. With the emergence of private farmers, previous
laws, regulating the allocation of irrigation water by WEs to agricultural cooperatives and state farms, have
become obsolete (para 1.16). A comprehensive Irrigation Code is required and has been drafted by
MoAF. On the basis of this Code, WUA Regulations (prepared by a consultant financed under the IDA
Critical Imports Project, CIP FY92) would be enacted as a decision by the Minister of Agriculture and
Food. Furthermore, a national Water Code has been drafted, under financing by the CIP, setting the
principles for allocation of water resources by all users including agriculture, industry, and energy. Once
the Water Code will have been discussed by the various ministries involved, the Irrigation Code would
come under the provisions of the Water Code. However, given the complexity and far-reaching
implications of the Water Code for the various water users, finalization of the Water Code is expected to
take considerable time. The Irrigation Code should, therefore, be enacted prior to the Water Code such
as to provide the urgently needed legal framework for irrigation and drainage.
1.28  Elements of the Irrigation Code.  IDA has reviewed the draft Irrigation Code.  After its
finalization, the key elements of the Irrigation Code are:
(a) Role of Water Enterprises. State-owned WEs will deliver water to the tertiary level where WUAs
or other large agricultural water users (e.g. joint-ventures) will take delivery of the water. WEs
will be responsible for operation and maintenance of primary and secondary canals for irrigation
and drainage and related structures;
(b) Role of WUAs. WUAs will be responsible for collection of water requests and water charges from
farmers and payment to WEs. WUAs will be in charge of system operation and maintenance, and
of infrastructure investments, below the secondary canal level;
(c) Mode of establishmnent of WUAs. Establishment of WUAs will be a precondition for the allocation
of irrigation water by WEs which will not deliver water to individual smallholders. WUAs will
be created through vote of all concerned farmers in the respective area. Membership in WUAs will
be compulsory for all farmers in an area if the majority of farmers votes in favor of the creation
of a WUA. The area coverage for individual WUAs would be determined by farmers;
(d) Role of Government. Water and all irrigation and drainage infrastructures are the property of the
State which can turn over user rights or ownership to water users. MoAF will initially supervise
and regulate the activities of WEs, and will set water charges in accordance with principles
described above (para 1.26). In the medium term, WUAs of each respective district will take over
this supervisory role;
(e) Other provisions, including principles for small-system transfer to water users (incorporating the
provisions of the June 1993 Presidential Decree on this matter), and right-of-way and other
easement issues.
I. Environmental Issues
1.29  Water and Soil Contamination.  Surface water quality is generally good, but industrial and
domestic waste-water, along with liquid discharges and solid and hazardous wastes from mining and energy



- 11 -
operations, are causing pollution of some rivers and lakes which supply water for irrigation.8 Control of
surface water quality is the responsibility of the Institute of Hydrometeorology within the Academy of
Science, but only few control points have been established and the laboratory equipment for analysis is
inadequate. Groundwater and its potential contamination through misapplication of fertilizers, pesticides
and other agricultural chemicals is of subordinated importance for irrigation. However, contamination of
soils and of surface water resulting from agricultural runoff following inadequate use of fertilizer and agro-
chemicals is an issue for irrigated agriculture.9 A Law on Plant Protection dealing with pest management
has been passed by Parliament in early 1993,10 and detailed guidelines for the safe use of pesticides by
private farmers, acceptable to IDA, have been developed under financing under the CIP. A further
potential source of soil contamination in the coastal areas is water logging and soil salinisation resulting
from water losses in the irrigation distribution system and during field application, causing saline
groundwater to rise into the root zone of the crops. This is of special concern in areas where existing
drainage systems are inoperative. Research and control of soil contamination and soil salinity is carried
out by the Soil Institute under MoAF which lacks the technical equipment and budgetary funds to fulfill
this role adequately.
CHAPTER II. BANK INVOLVEMENT AND STRATEGY
A. Bankwide Lending to the Sector
2.1    Overall Experience. Irrigation and drainage projects account for about six percent of total Bank
lending and one-third of lending to agriculture. OED's 1992 Annual Review of Evaluation Results and
the 1993 OED Review of World Bank Irrigation Experience provide evidence on performance and
recurrent issues of some 200 Bank irrigation projects. Overall operational performance has been above
agricultural projects - while being below all Bank lending - but has in general been poorer than anticipated
at project appraisal. Critical factors for project success include: quality and adequacy of system design,
especially in wet-zone surface systems; quality of construction works; achieved levels of cost recovery;
quality of operation and maintenance; and unexpected shifts in output prices and macroeconomic
distortions. Borrowers often disengage too early from projects, particularly under program or time-slice
approaches, leaving irrigation and drainage systems incomplete and land development unfinished. For
many projects, complementary services (such as extension, research and marketing) associated with the
productivity of irrigation development have been inadequate or seriously delayed. Other issues leading to
poor performance are related to: underestimated costs frequently due to lack of sufficiently advanced
engineering design at appraisal; cost overruns due to delays in completing engineering designs, preparing
tender documents, and evaluating bids; slower-than-estimated civil works construction and equipment
delivery times; and lower-than-estimated water use efficiencies due to deterioration of the infrastructure.
2.2    Cost Recovery, Maintenance Quality and Farmer Participation. The record of cost recovery
in Bank-financed irrigation projects has been unsatisfactory. In most cases, no capital costs were recovered
8/     Polluted rivers include the Shkumbin river below Elbasan for 30 km (effluent from iron and mineral industry), the Seman river
below Gjanica for 10 km (oil industry waste), Goanica (oil industry waste), the Mat river after joining of Fani river (copper
industry waste), and the Kiri river below Shkoder for 8 km (various industrial wastes).
9/     See Report No. 11784-ALB: Albania - Environmental Strategy Study.
10/    Law No. 7662 of January 19, 1993.



- 12 -
and in two-thirds of reviewed projects the covenant requiring that cost recovery fully satisfy funding for
system operation and maintenance had not been met,"1 with the proportion of respective costs recovered
generally reaching only a range of 15 to 45 percent. Inadequate operation and maintenance is generally
attributed to such poor cost recovery. However, the 1993 OED Review suggests that improved cost
recovery will only result in better system performance if revenues from water charges are clearly
earmarked for operation and maintenance (financial link) and if service-providing irrigation agencies are
directly accountable to water users (incentives link). Furthermore, entities collecting water fees must be
able to use them directly to oversee operation and maintenance (financial autonomy). The latter often
implies financial decentralization of irrigation agencies from the central government budget, and partial or
total transfer of responsibility for fee collection and system operation and maintenance to water user groups
at the farmer level. These experiences have been taken into explicit consideration in the design of the
proposed project.
B. Lending to Agriculture in Albania
2.3    Irrigation Component under the Critical Imports Project. The first IDA operation in Albania
was the Critical Imports Project (CIP, FY92, total cost US$55 million) which includes an Irrigation
Component of US$6.8 million financing equipment, spare parts and materials for selected repairs of the
infrastructure and providing consultant services to set up WUAs on a pilot basis and to draft a Water Code
and regulations for WUAs (see Annex 3). An irrigation command area of 18,455 ha has benefitted from
resulting rehabilitation works by end-1993, a further 43,000 ha is planned to be affected by rehabilitation
works by mid-1994. An important lesson learned from this operation is the importance to ensure that
sufficient budgetary provisions are available for WEs to cover the cost of locally procured items such as
cement for construction works and fuel for vehicle operation.12 Experience from establishing the first
WUAs in the coastal districts of the country shows that farmers are generally keen to join into water user
groups if this guarantees more reliable water supply by WEs and better maintenance of systems below the
secondary level. Local agronomists of the extension service network under MoAF maintain a good contact
with private farmers and appear to be well suited to promote WUAs. WEs are equally interested in the
creation of WUAs in order to wholesale irrigation water to them and to collect water fees from them,
rather than having to deal with individual farmers. Villages are the only widely accepted basis for WUAs,
and farmers see an opportunity to develop WUAs in parallel with recently emerging, largely village-based
Private Farmers Associations which are aiming at common supply of inputs and joint marketing of produce
(see survey results in Annex 5.5).13
2.4     Agricultural Credit. The two other agricultural IDA projects in Albania deal, inter alia, with the
reform of the agricultural credit system to adapt it to the financial needs of the private farm sector. Under
the Rural Poverty Alleviation Pilot Project (RPAPP, FY93, total cost US$4.8 million), a bottom-up
approach is being followed by creating a network of village-based credit funds in selected districts.
11/    The three main reasons for non-compliance have been: unreliable water supply due to poor operation and maintenance; the
often heavy burden of direct and indirect taxes already imposed by governments on the farming sector; and lack of
government commitment to recover costs. See World Bank Lending Conditionality: A Review of Cost Recovery in Irrigation
Projects, OED, OPS No. 6283, 1986
12/    As of end-1993, WEs have spent Lek 5.2 million in local costs for the purchase of cement (888 tons), and Lek 23.7 million
for the purchase of fuel (812 tons), in order to carry out rehabilitation works under the CIP.
13/    As of July 31, 1993, a total of 90 Private Farmers Associations have been established, covering up to 300 ha per association,
with an average holding of 40 ha. The large majority of these associations is located along the coastal lowland areas.



- 13 -
Decisions concerning the allocation of small loans to farmers and other village members are taken strictly
at the local level, and loan demand has been strong since the start of project implementation in April 1993.
The proposed follow-up Rural Development Project (FY95) will expand the village credit concept to the
national level. Under the Agriculture Sector Adjustment Credit (ASAC, FY93, total cost US$52 million),
in implementation since September 1993, the establishment of a new Rural Commercial Bank is being
supported through key technical assistance (US$3 million) and a line of credit to the Government for on-
lending to private farmers through the new bank (US$5 million). These IDA operations lay the ground
for providing farmers with seasonal and investment loans to purchase necessary farm inputs and to finance
on-farm investments into the irrigation infrastructure in order to maximize yields and output resulting from
improved irrigation.
2.5    Agricultural Extension.  Until end-1991, MoAF employed some 15,000 extension service
specialists in various agricultural disciplines which assisted agricultural cooperatives and state farms in the
management of their operations. In early 1992, Government issued a directive that these specialists will
form commune-based advisory groups (one group for three to four villages) to assist the emerging private
farmers. The future role and scope of agricultural extension in Albania requires further analysis to ensure
high responsiveness of advisory services to the needs of smallholder farmers.  For that purpose, a
comprehensive Extension Master Plan is currently in preparation through financing under ASAC.
2.6    Marketing and Distribution. Most private farmers are still engaged in subsistence agriculture with
very limited marketable surplus production. With an increase in productivity, following improved
irrigation, the current obstacles to marketing and distribution will become apparent, including the lack of
access to export markets, low domestic purchasing power, a general lack of market price information, and
inadequate transport and wholesale facilities. The proposed Dairy Marketing Project (FY95) would address
some of these issues and promote cooperative structures of milk collection and processing. The on-going
privatization of domestic agro-industries is expected to raise demand for local agricultural output and to
create new marketing opportunities for farmers. Joint marketing of farmers' surplus production will be
promoted further through the increasing number of Private Farmers Associations.
2.7    Land Consolidation. Given the small size of individual farms and the fragmentation into several
isolated small plots, land consolidation is an important issue for more efficient land use and, thus, to
achieve higher yields under improved irrigation. Under ASAC, Government will enact legislation, by mid-
1994, to allow for long-term leasing and sub-leasing of former cooperative land by the now private owners.
2.8    Feeder Roads. A total of 870km of non-paved roads under the administration by the Ministry of
Transport are located in the seven districts forming the project area under the proposed Irrigation
Rehabilitation Project.'4 An additional estimated 1,000km of dirt roads are under administration by local
authorities. These roads would be repaired, as necessary, under a proposed Feeder Roads Project (FY95)
which will focus on the rehabilitation of non-paved communal, village and feeder roads, primarily in
irrigated areas in the coastal plains.
C. Sector Strategy and Bank Support
2.9    Government's Objectives. IDA supports Government's overall objectives in the agriculture sector:
sustained growth through improvements in yields and productivity, conversion from subsistence agriculture
14/    Total length of non-paved roads per district is as follows: Laci 61km, Kruje 91km, Tirana 208km, Durres 97km, Kavaje
57km, Lushnje 64km, Fier 289km.



- 14 -
towards marketing of surplus on domestic and export markets, reduction of rural poverty and prevention
of rural-urban migration through creation of employment opportunities in rural areas, and food security.
Government is fully aware that irrigated and drained agriculture will be a cornerstone to achieve these
objectives since 80 percent of agricultural production stems from areas under irrigation command.
Therefore, Government aims at restoring system delivery capacities quickly; ensuring the sustainability of
infrastructure investments; promoting the involvement of private farmers in modernizing and maintaining
on-farm structures; and, eventually, expanding irrigation to not covered high-potential areas.
2.10   Sector Strategy.  To achieve these objectives, Government has developed a sectoral strategy
framework for irrigation and drainage with a focus on the following seven central issues and objectives,
as set out in table 2. 1: (a) rehabilitation and modernization of the physical infrastructure, under active
private sector involvement for on-farm investments; (b) ensuring farmer participation in system operation
and maintenance through creation of WUAs and eventual transfer of small systems to water users; (c)
strengthening and regulating state-owned WEs while terminating CEs' monopoly on irrigation construction
works; (d) introducing cost-covering water charges for water users and ensuring the financial autonomy
of WEs; (e) monitoring and protecting surface water and soil quality; (f) ensuring the availability of
agricultural support services and of required rural infrastructure; and (g) strengthening the engineering
design and project implementation capacity at the MoAF and WE level. Required actions to implement
this strategy would be supported by various bilateral and multilateral donors, with IDA taking a leading
role in coordinating this external assistance provided to the sector.  The project closely reflects the
development priorities formulated in the Country Assistance Strategy for Albania, as discussed by the
Board of Directors of IDA on September 14, 1993: rehabilitation of the basic infrastructure, involving
rehabilitation of existing equipment, new investments, and adequate cost recovery; and support for the
development of private agriculture through improvements in the infrastructure and creation of proper
pricing incentives.
2.11   First IDA  Operation in Irrigation.  IDA's strategy is to support Government in its above-
mentioned sectoral strategy and objectives through a phased lending program of consecutive operations.
Following the Irrigation Component under the Critical Imports Project (para 2.3), the proposed Irrigation
Rehabilitation Project would be the first major IDA operation in Albania's irrigation and drainage sector. 5
Under this operation, IDA would finance technically straightforward and economically high-yielding
rehabilitation works in selected high-potential areas and systems. A modern demonstration scheme for
canal flow control and demonstrations of efficient techniques for on-farm water management would also
be financed. In addition, IDA would provide assistance to initiate key institutional reforms, including a
program for the promotion and establishment of Water User Associations in selected districts to broaden
and institutionalize farmer participation in irrigation management. Technical and managerial assistance
would be made available for state-owned Water Enterprises to improve their operating efficiency.
Finalization of the new legal and regulatory framework for irrigation management would be supported
through technical assistance. Furthermore, IDA would support the formulation of fundamental policy
reforms, most importantly the adoption of cost-covering water charges aiming at full recovery of recurrent
and investment costs, thus reducing the fiscal burden of operating, maintaining and investing into the
infrastructure. Decentralization and private sector involvement in irrigation management would be fostered
under the proposed project through formulation of a program and timetable to transfer small systems to
private irrigators. The domestic construction industry would benefit from the proposed IDA operation
through bidding for rehabilitation works contracts suitable to small private contractors.
15/    Rehabilitation of small irrigation systems are also eligible for financing under the infrastructure and rural works component
of the IDA-financed Rural Poverty Alleviation Pilot Project (FY93).



- 15 -
2.12  Donor Coordination. The proposed project would be closely coordinated with other donors
supporting the irrigation sector in Albania. Under the IFAD Mountain Areas Irrigated Agriculture
Development Project (total cost US$18 million), irrigation systems would be rehabilitated in three low-
income mountain districts (Bulqize, Diber and Mirdite). The objective of rural development under the
IFAD project complements the proposed IDA operation which would not include the three districts covered
by IFAD. The Integrated Agricultural Development Project financed by the Dutch Government in the Fier
district aims at rehabilitating the irrigation system in one selected commune (2,500 ha total command area)
and this area would be excluded from repair works under the proposed IDA operation. A project proposal
financed by the French Government for a pilot irrigation program in the Korce district envisages
modernization of on-farm distribution systems on 1,000 ha under irrigation command. This proposal would
not be supported under the IDA project since cost estimates for rehabilitation works are very high (up to
US$10,000 per ha) and are, therefore, incompatible with the approach followed under the IDA project.
2.13  Future IDA Involvement. After implementation of the first project, IDA-financed follow-up
operations in the sector would broaden the scope of infrastructure investments by extending system
rehabilitation and possibly completion works for unfinished schemes to economically viable systems in all
areas of the country. WUAs would be established on all irrigated areas and be consolidated into larger
groups to represent more closely hydraulic units. WEs would gradually be brought under the direct
supervision and control of water users. Irrigation management would be integrated into a larger framework
of water resources management at the basin level. IDA would continue to focus heavily on institutional
development and policy reforms to ensure cost recovery and to guarantee the financial sustainability of
investments. Future IDA projects would reflect key lessons on cost recovery and farmer participation
learned during the first operation. Continued IDA support in a phased manner will be necessary given the
comprehensive institutional and policy adjustments required and the role IDA can play in coordinating
external donor support for the irrigation sector.

CHAPTER III. THE PROJECT
A. Project Rationale and Area
3.1    Background. In the spring of 1992, the Government of Albania expressed interest in World Bank
Group assistance to rehabilitate and restructure the irrigation and drainage sector. A first mission, carried
out by the FAO/World Bank Cooperative Programme, resulted in a comprehensive Irrigation Subsector
Review in the summer of 1992. A second FAO mission identified the project and delivered a Project
Preparation Report in spring 1993. IDA missions in the fall and winter of 1993 followed to finalize the
preparation of the project.
3.2    Project Rationale.  The technical, organizational and policy challenges towards adapting the
irrigation sector to the new structure of smallholder agriculture are substantial. At the same time, available
budgetary resources are scarce and the capacity to implement required institutional changes is limited.
Thus, rehabilitation and expansion of all systems to achieve optimal flow capacities is not a realistic short-
term objective, and a choice needs to be made as to which type of infrastructure investments would be
included under the proposed project. To maximize economic benefits, Government and IDA agree that
this first project in Albania's irrigation sector will concentrate on the rehabilitation of the existing irrigation
and drainage infrastructure to restore as-designed capacities quickly. Improvements in the conveyance
system will be undertaken only in selected cases where the net benefit of expanding delivery capacities is
very high. Private surface pumping and private groundwater development are options for individual
farmers who can seek credit financing from rural financial institutions supported under two IDA operations
(para 2.4) and, therefore, would not be financed by the proposed project. An extensive modernization of
systems aiming at correcting insufficient water supply and improving flow control would be left to a further
stage of development when economically justified by an increase in cropping intensities, by a clear shift
towards cash crops, and after the establishment and functioning of WUAs. In addition to infrastructure
investments, the project will support institution building and key policy reforms through investments,
technical assistance and training.
3.3    Project Area. A further choice relates to the question whether rehabilitation works would be
concentrated on several districts and schemes or be scattered over the entire country. Since it is the
objective of Government to maximize the project impact on agricultural production and farm income,
rehabilitation works will be carried out exclusively in areas with high agro-potential located in the fertile
coastal plains. A further selection criterion for the project area are demonstrated good prospects for farmer
participation in establishing WUAs. The experience under the Irrigation Component of the Critical Imports
Project (para 2.3) provides evidence of strong interest in setting up WUAs among those farmers that are
located along the coastal districts where financial returns from irrigation are high. Based on theses criteria,
selected irrigation and drainage systems in a total of seven adjacent districts along the coastal plains will
form the project area for rehabilitation works. As set out in table 3.1, an incremental area of 28,467 ha
will return to irrigation while 45,019 ha with currently partly operational systems will receive improved
irrigation under the project. The incremental area for drainage is equal to 17,935 ha, with an additional
area of 81,369 ha benefitting from improved drainage. The project area represents 51 percent of the
irrigation command area and 58 percent of the drainage command area in the seven districts.
3.4    Selection of Schemes. Schemes to be rehabilitated under the project have been selected following
a least cost-per-ha approach within each district (see Annexes 4.2 and 4.3). The project would focus on
the rehabilitation of large irrigation and drainage schemes to maximize the economic impact of works.
Small irrigation schemes of less than 100 ha, mostly located in hill areas and involving high pump lift of



- 20 -
more than 50m, would be excluded from works under the project. Only drainage schemes associated with
a selected irrigation scheme would be rehabilitated under the project. Some 58 percent of the irrigation
and 56 percent of drainage schemes included under the project cover more than 1,000 ha per scheme,
accounting for 94 percent and 96 percent of the project rehabilitation area for irrigation and drainage,
respectively (see Annexes 2.3 and 2.4).

Note:  Incremental areas are command zones which are currently not irrigated/drained, and which will return to full
irrigation/drainage under the project. Improved areas are currently partly irrigated/drained and will become fully
operational under the project. Benefit areas represent 100% of incremental areas plus 30% of improved areas.
3.5    International Waterways. One of the 21 irrigation schemes to be rehabilitated under the project,
in the southern district of Fier, is located along the Vjoses River which is an international waterway, within
the meaning of OD 7.50, entering Albania from its south-eastern neighbor Greece, and leaving Albania
into the Adriatic Sea. Two-thirds of the catchment area of this river lie within Albania, the rest lies in
Greece. The irrigation scheme in question, the so-called Vjoses-Levan-Fier (VFL) scheme, covers an
irrigated area of 28,000 ha, of which 9,500 ha would benefit from rehabilitation works under the project
(see Map No. IBRD 25359, scheme code FRU1). The scheme takes about 50 percent of the available
water in the Vjoses River. In accordance with OD 7.50, a notification of Greece for the activities under
the project is not required for the following two reasons: (a) the project will finance only rehabilitation
and upgrading of existing systems. As the VFL scheme is located downstream from the other riparian,
it will not have any adverse affect on the quantity of water flows to others, nor will water quality be
affected as a result of the rehabilitation works. In addition, the project will not involve any increase in
water extraction; and (b) a review of international agreements and arrangements that exist between Albania
and Greece on the sharing of international waters that might be affected by the project has shown that none
of these agreements would require notification for the activities to be carried out under the project.



- 21 -
B. Project Objectives and Components
3.6   The proposed project would have the following three main objectives:
(a) to increase agricultural production by raising farm productivity through rapid rehabilitation of
the existing irrigation and drainage infrastructure;
(b) to alleviate poverty by increasing income of small private farmers and creating employment
opportunities for dependent farm labor in rural areas;
(c) to ensure the long-term sustainability of irrigation and drainage investments through farmer
participation in operation and maintenance, efficient system management and adequate cost
recovery.
3.7   To support the above objectives, the project, to be implemented over a period of seven years
(January 1, 1994, initiated under the PPF, to December 31, 2000), would consist of the following four
components:
(a) system rehabilitation of 21 irrigation schemes and 18 drainage schemes, covering a command area
of 73,486 ha for irrigation and 99,304 ha for drainage in seven coastal districts, and including a
demonstration scheme for canal control covering 1,000 ha of irrigation command area;
(b) institutional development and capacity building, involving equipment, technical assistance and
training for improved system operation and maintenance, enhancement of the domestic design
capacity, establishment of WUAs, and for an on-farm water management program;
(c) policy support, through studies and programs, including the regulatory framework for irrigation,
small system transfer, WUA consolidation, setting of water charges, and the enabling environment
for private investments into surface and groundwater pumping; and
(d) project implementation support through provision of office equipment, technical assistance and
training.
C. Detailed Features
Component 1: System Rehabilitation (Base Cost US$31.5 million)
3.8    Area Coverage. The project would rehabilitate: (a) 21 irrigation schemes on a total irrigated area
of 73,486 ha, with an average coverage of 3,500 ha per system, at a total base cost of US$19.9 million;
and (b) 18 drainage schemes on a total command area of 99,304 ha, with an average coverage of 5,500
ha per system, at a total base cost of US$8.1 million. Under the project, 28,467 ha of currently non-
irrigated land, and 17,935 ha of non-drained land, would return to irrigation and drainage, while 45,019
ha and 81,369 ha would benefit from improved irrigation and drainage, respectively. The average cost
of rehabilitation works, net of engineering costs, would be US$470 per ha for irrigation and US$190 per



- 22 -
ha for drainage (see Annexes 4.2 and 4.3). 16 All schemes to be rehabilitated under the project have been
identified and technically appraised during project preparation. Within the scope of Annual Work Plans
for all rehabilitation works (see para 4.3 below), irrigation schemes covering more than 5,000 ha
(accounting for 24 percent of the number of schemes, covering 54 of the project area) would be reevaluated
on a yearly basis, following a periodic update of cost estimates, before procurement of civil works would
be initiated.
3.9    Cost of Civil Works and Pumping Equipment. Rehabilitation works financed under the proposed
project would be of a straightforward technical nature. They would include: earthworks for drainage (base
cost of US$1.4 million); canal cleaning (US$6.9 million); repair or reconstruction of canal linings (US$4.7
million); replacement and installation of structures and gates (US$2.0 million, excluding the demonstration
scheme for canal flow control); rehabilitation of culverts and access roads (US$4.1 million); and the
replacement of electrical and mechanical equipment for pumping irrigation and drainage water (cost of
US$8.9 million). To prepare for a future upgrading of the infrastructure, modern technology of flow
control in primary and large canals would be introduced on a pilot basis on one selected system (see para
3. 11 below) while improvements in flow control and conveyance on minor canals would be implemented
wherever feasible under minimal incremental cost. Any rehabilitation works below the secondary canal
level would require the prior establishment of WUAs on the respective benefit areas in order to ensure
safeguarding of investments and to guarantee adequate system operation and maintenance.
3.10   Design and Supervision of Works.   Major works would be designed and supervised by the
Tirana-based Institute for Study and Design of Water Works (ISDWW) while district-based Water
Enterprises (WEs) would be responsible for engineering and supervision of minor works. Some US$20.2
million worth of rehabilitation works would require design and supervision which would be shared between
ISDWW (US$10.4 million of major works) and WEs (US$9.8 million of minor works). At an upper fee
limit of 4 percent calculated on the base cost of rehabilitation works, the total base cost for design and
supervision would equal US$810,000, which would be split between ISDWW and the seven WEs in the
project area (see Annex 5.4 for a detailed cost breakdown).'7
3.11   Demonstration Scheme for Canal Control. At a total base cost of US$0.6 million, the project
would support the construction of a demonstration scheme for a selected sector covering a command area
of 1,000 ha within an existing irrigation scheme included in system rehabilitation works under the project.
Canal design would be modified as required to allow for installation of state-of-the-art canal control
technology for water level and flow control. The incremental cost for investments into control structures
and gates would be US$400 per ha, or US$0.4 million for the complete demonstration scheme. This pilot
scheme would be used for training of Albanian irrigation system designers and operators. Design,
procurement and construction works for the demonstration scheme would be carried out over a three-year
period. The project would also finance 8 m/months of technical assistance and related training at a cost
of US$0.2 million (see Annex 5).
16/    Per-ha costs are related to the estimated benefit area which has been calculated as the sum of incremental command areas
returning to irrigation and drainage, plus 30 percent of command areas receiving improved irrigation and drainage under the
project.
17/    If private engineering firms were engaged, the commercial fee rate for design and supervision of works would be equal to an
international average of about 7 percent. Given the budgetary support for ISDWW and WEs through provision of vehicles
and engineering equipment under the project, a reduced fee of 4 percent for engineering and supervision has been estimated
