3.12   On-Farm Repairs. Farmers would be expected to undertake repairs at the tertiary and quaternary
canal level to rehabilitate small structures and canals on their farms. It is estimated that incremental on-
farm investments under the project would equal US$50 equivalent per ha of benefit area over the
implementation period of the project, resulting in a total base cost of US$2.1 million.
Component 2: Institutional Development (Base Cost US$5.5 million)
3.13   System Operation and Maintenance (US$3.6 million). WEs received emergency equipment for
limited repairs and maintenance of systems under the CIP (para 2.3). In order to improve WEs'
operational efficiency and to enhance the quality of their services to farmers, Government would broaden
this support for seven WEs located in the project area through an equity contribution at a total investment
cost of US$2.4 million. This support would include basic rehabilitation works for workshops and
buildings, and the provision of selected canal maintenance machinery (excavators, cement mixers and
workshop equipment), vehicles, and office and communication equipment, at a combined cost of US$1.8
million. Furthermore, 26 m/months of technical assistance and a comprehensive training program for WE
staff would be supported in the areas of water distribution, subsurface drainage, maintenance of pumping
equipment, and cost accounting, at a combined cost of US$0.6 million (see Annex 5). It is estimated that
incremental recurrent cost for system operation and maintenance in the project area would equal
US$600,000 per year over the implementation period of the project.'8 Since the full recovery of recurrent
cost borne by WEs would be achieved through an annual increase of water charges to cost-covering levels
by year five of the project, an average of 50 percent of incremental recurrent cost resulting from the
project, equivalent to US$300,000 per year over a four-year period or a total cost of US$1.2 million
equivalent, would require financing under the project through transfers from the Government budget (see
paras 5.8 through 5. 10 below). Arrears in energy charges that have been billed to the seven WEs in the
project area by KESH, the Albanian power utility, have been cleared prior to the presentation of the project
to the Board of Directors of IDA.
3.14 Design Capacity (US$0.4 million). Both ISDWW and WEs have been supported under the CIP
through provision of vehicles, office and engineering equipment.  To enable them  to fulfill their
responsibilities of works design and supervision under the project, their engineering capacity would be
strengthened additionally through an equity contribution by Government, financed under the project,
through provision of required technical equipment and vehicles (US$200,000, of which US$105,000 would
be allocated to WEs), and of 3 m/months of technical assistance in conjunction with a comprehensive
training program (US$200,000) (see Annex 5). Financing of this component has been initiated under the
Project Preparation Facility (PPF), approved in September 1993.
3.15   Establishment of WUAs (US$1.2 million). Farmer participation in the operation and maintenance
of irrigation systems has been successfully initiated through the election of village irrigation representatives
and the creation of WUAs in coastal districts on a pilot basis under the CIP (paras 1.14 and 2.3). The
proposed project would expand these activities through a five-year program for the establishment of WUAs
on all irrigation schemes to be rehabilitated under the project. WUAs would initially be established on a
village basis, covering between 50 and 400 ha, with an average membership after full establishment of
about 200 farmers and a minimum initial size of 15 members. The seven districts in the project area
18/    These costs are broken down into US$ 400,000 for incremental salaries (3 engineers, 6 technicians and 100 workers per WE)
and US$ 200,000 for incremental vehicle operating cost, including maintenance of construction machinery. Incremental energy
costs, resulting from repair and replacement of pumping equipment, would be fully recovered from collected water charges.



- 24 -
comprise a total of 536 villages'9 in 94 communes, and up to 100,000 farmers would be included under
the program. WUAs would be established based on WUA Regulation, developed under the CIP and to
be enacted under the project as a decision by the Minister of Agriculture and Food (see para 3.17 below).
To establish WUAs, decentralized WUA Units would be created in each WE located in the project area.
These Units would be staffed with qualified local WUA promoters who would include local agronomists
of the extension service network under the District Department of Agriculture to disseminate information
on water user groups, to assist farmers in the organization and legal creation of WUAs, and to provide
continuous long-term counseling to WUAs. WUA Units would be reduced in size after the first three
project years and be consolidated to one central unit by the end of the project. Over a six-year period, the
project would finance vehicles and office equipment for WUA Units (US$130,000), about 4,300 m/months
of local WUA promoters in WUA Units (US$430,000), and 36 m/months of technical assistance and
training by expatriate experts (US$610,000), using the extensive experience gained in various other
countries with respect to sustainable farmer participation in local irrigation management (see Annex 5).
Financing of this component has been initiated under the PPF.
3.16 On-Farm Water Management (US$0.3 million). To reduce on-farm water losses during irrigation
and to increase water distribution efficiency below the tertiary level, the project would include a pilot
program for water management at the farm level. The program would consist in on-farm and off-farm
demonstrations of simple techniques for land levelling and inexpensive methods for lining of quaternary
canals. Further components would include instruction in modern smallholder irrigation techniques and
adequate irrigation scheduling. Applied research required for this pilot program would be conducted by
the Soil Institute on two existing experimental stations in Lushnje and Tirana. Field demonstrations would
be carried out by staff of the agricultural extension service on selected pilot farms in the seven districts of
the rehabilitation area covered under the project. These demonstrations would be combined with instruction
on the safe handling and application of fertilizers and pesticides, in accordance with IDA guidelines on
integrated pest management, using manuals and training material developed under the Critical Imports
Project.  The project would provide financing for the required scientific, demonstration, office and
transport equipment (US$130,000) as well as for 6 m/months of technical assistance with related training
by local and expatriate trainers (US$180,000) (see Annex 5).
Component 3: Policy Support (Base Cost US$0.3 million)
3.17  Regulatory Framework for Irrigation. Finalization of the draft Irrigation Code as the required
legal basis and regulatory framework of irrigation management is of high priority and will be supported
under the project through provision of 3 m/months of expert advice from an experienced expatriate
specialist (US$60,000, see Annex 5. 1). Financing of this policy support component has already been
initiated under the PPF. The Irrigation Code would define the role of WEs, WUAs and of Government
and would include additional provisions related, inter alia, to small-system transfer and right-of-way issues
(see para 1.28). The Irrigation Code has been passed by the Albanian Parliament prior to presentation of
the project to the Board of Directors of IDA. Following the passage of the Irrigation Code, WUA
Regulations would be enacted through an implementing decision by the Minister of Agriculture and Food.
Enactment of the WUA Regulations would be a condition of effectiveness of the IDA Credit.
3.18  Small System  Transfer.  Albania's irrigation system  is highly fragmented; operation and
maintenance through WEs of pumping stations and reservoirs serving the smallest independent schemes
19/   The number of villages per district is as follows: Laci 20, Kruje 35, Tirana 150, Durres 52, Kavaje 62. Lushnje 107, Fier
109.



- 25 -
is costly on a per-ha basis, and the economic viability of many micro-systems is questionable (para 1.21).
Decentralizing irrigation management through complete turnover of small systems to private farmers would
reduce related costs through more efficient local management and would allow for an internalization of
costs at the user level. The June 1993 Presidential Decree on this matter is a first step in this direction
(see footnote 7). The project would provide funding for 3 mi/months of technical assistance for a study
(US$60,000, see Annex 5. 1) to develop a program and timetable for the transfer of ownership and
management responsibility for small systems to WUAs within the project area for rehabilitation works.
The study would take into consideration the range of different models of transfer and local management
of irrigation systems applied in various other countries. It would lay out the expected effects of ownership
and management transfer on farmers' productivity and profitability, implications on WEs' budgets, and the
prospects of financial and economic viability and sustainability of small systems. The study would also
deal with aspects of equity, rural development and the overall social impact of system transfer. Given the
variety of major institutional reforms to be addressed under the proposed project, implementation of the
program of small system transfer would be kept for a follow-up project.
3.19  WUA Consolidation. After their initial establishment on the basis of village boundaries, WUAs
would be grouped into larger organizations, such as water user boards or WUA unions, in order to more
closely represent hydraulic units that are served by a one single source of water. These organizations
would take over additional operation and maintenance responsibilities for secondary canals and for related
flow control structures, thus providing for an even stronger, decentralized farmer participation in irrigation
management. The project would finance 3 m/months of technical assistance for a study (US$60,000, see
Annex 5.1) to prepare a detailed program with timetable to promote the consolidation of WUAs in the
project area for rehabilitation works. As in the case of small system transfer, implementation of the
program for WUA consolidation would be kept for follow-up operations after village-based WUAs will
have been created and have operated successfully for some time.
3.20  Water Charges. Although irrigation water is being priced efficiently on a volumetric basis, current
water charges are set at below cost levels. Present charges levels require an initial increase and periodic
subsequent adjustments to achieve the recovery (a) of actual district-based recurrent costs of system
operation and maintenance borne by WEs, and (b) of investment costs into system rehabilitation borne by
the central Government budget (see para 5.8 below). Under the project, 5 m/months of technical assistance
for a respective study would be financed (US$100,000, see Annex 5.1) to establish a comprehensive
database with subsequent design and implementation of a financial model for determining cost-covering
levels of water charges, and to explore alternative models for water tariff differentiation by district and
scheme and by cost components for investment and recurrent costs.
3.21   Private Pumping Development. Private surface pumping and groundwater development through
shallow tubewells are attractive investment options for smallholder farmers and are eligible for financing
through rural financial institutions supported under two IDA operations (para 2.4). To promote private
investments in this area, the project would finance 2 man/months of technical assistance for such a study
(US$40,000, see Annex 5.1) addressing potential legal and institutional constraints in the enabling
environment that presently limit farmers' access to irrigation water stemming from both private surface and
groundwater pumping.
Component 4: Project Implementation Support (Base Cost US$0.8 million)
3.22    Project Management Capacity. The proposed project would have a major focus on upgrading
the institutional capacity of MoAF for preparing, implementing, monitoring and assessing of projects in



- 26 -
irrigation and drainage. For that purpose, a special Project Management Unit (PMU) was established in
December 1993, under the direct supervision of the Minister of Agriculture and Food. PMU would be
strengthened under the project through: (i) provision of office equipment and vehicles at a cost of
US$1 10,000; (ii) a total of 25 m/months of technical assistance and training in the areas of technical
designs and tender documents for civil works, procurement, disbursements and financial controlling, review
of Annual Works Plans, and project impact assessment, including annual external audits of project
accounts, at cost of US$610,000; and (iii) through financing of incremental salaries and vehicle operating
costs of US$80,000 (see Annexes 4.6 and 5.1). Financing of this component has been initiated under the
PPF and through support from a Japanese Grant.
D. Project Cost and Financing
3.23   Total Project Costs.  Table 3.2 provides a summary for the total project costs, including
contingencies, of US$44.1 million equivalent, broken down by project components. Civil works and
pumping equipment account for a combined cost of US$31.5 million or 83 percent of total base costs.
Technical assistance, training and studies in all project components (total of 128 m/months of expatriate
experts and 4,300 mlmonths of local experts) account for US$3.1 million and represent 8 percent of total
base costs. Cost data include applicable duties and local taxes, and assurances have been obtained from
Government during negotiations that any duties and local taxes be covered through the Government budget.
3.24   Cost Estimates. Costs for construction works under the project were estimated based on surveys
carried out by Water Enterprises in each concerned district among local private contractors. Detailed unit
costs for all works, materials and equipment have been estimated on a district basis (see Annex 4.4). Cost
estimates are expressed in US$ equivalent terms in order to reduce the danger of distortions resulting from
inflation and exchange rate fluctuations. Local costs are expressed in US$ at the exchange rate prevailing
at the end of October 1993 (US$1.00 = Lek 100). Physical contingencies for construction works have
been set at 10 percent over the estimated base costs, while the remaining project components, including
recurrent costs, have been allocated a 5 percent cost allowance for physical increase. Price contingencies
have been set assuming an annual international rate of dollar inflation of 2.5 percent.

3.25   IDA Credit and Cofinancing. Table 3.3 presents the financing plan for the proposed project.
Government, private farmers and IDA would cover 45 percent of total project cost, the balance would be
covered by cofinancing. Proceeds from the IDA credit would be used for the rehabilitation of five gravity-
based irrigation schemes (total benefit area of 12,451 ha) and three drainage schemes (total benefit area
of 17,868 ha) in the three districts of Kavaja, Lushnje and Fier20. IDA would also finance all maintenance
machinery, vehicles, laboratory and office equipment, and selected key technical assistance for institutional
development (see Annex 7.2). Strong interest for cofinancing has been expressed by a number of
cofinanciers, tentative amounts are indicated in Table 3.3. During negotiations, assurances have been
obtained that the Government would provide funding to cover any financing gap in the event that sufficient
cofinancing could not be secured by October 31, 1995. IDA would retain the right to suspend the project,
or any parts of it, after that date in the event that neither sufficient cofinancing nor additional budgetary
funds to close the cofinancing gap would be available.
E. Procurement
3.26 Packaging of Civil Works Contracts. To promote economy and efficiency of procurement,
rehabilitation works would be grouped and packaged in 211 civil works contracts. Of these, 12 contracts
are estimated to cost the equivalent of US$250,000 or more each, although, depending on cofinancing
arrangements, it is expected that IDA-financed works contracts would not exceed this threshold. The bulk
of contracts would not exceed US$100,000 equivalent each.  The average contract size would be
US$98,000 for irrigation-related works and US$64,000 for works related to drainage rehabilitation. Out
of a total cost for civil works of US$26.7 million (including contingencies), IDA would finance an
aggregate contract volume of US$4.5 million, of which US$3.0 million would be for canal cleaning and
lining, and US$1.5 million for control structures and gates. Annexes 6.1 and 6.2 give an indicative
breakdown of procurement packages for rehabilitation works under the project. The procurement of civil
works would be carried out in the following manner:
20/    IDA-financed schemes include the following irrigation systems: Peqin-Kavaja Canal (Kavaja, code KJU-lg); Okshtun
Reservoir (Kajava, code KJU-2); Thanes Reservoir (Lushnje, code LUU-lg); Vjoses-Levan-Fier Canal (Fier, code FRU-lg);
and Libofsha Field (Fier, code FRU-2g). Drainage schemes financed by IDA include: Kavaja Plain (Kavaja, code KJK-2g);
Emissary Myzeqese (Lushnje, code LUK-1g); and Sheqit Plain (Fier, code FRK-3g).



- 28 -
3.27  Procurement of Civil Works Contracts. Works contracts up to US$250,000 would be procured
through Local Competitive Bidding (LCB) in accordance with procedures and documentation agreed with
IDA. LCB documents developed by IDA for the FY94 Housing Project would be adapted for this purpose.
The expected aggregate IDA-financed contract volume in this category equals US$3 million for canal
cleaning and lining, for a total of about 20 contracts. During negotiations assurances have been obtained
from Government that the PMU would use the document - Invitation to Quote and Contract Conditions for
Procurement under Local Competitive Bidding - for award of contracts up to US$250,000 equivalent each.
If required, government would finance very small works contracts that are scattered or located in remote
areas of the project through force account.
3.28  Local Civil Works Contractors. These contractors, whose number in the spring of 1994 exceeded
100 for the Tirana region alone and is growing constantly, would be able to provide sufficient competition
for the above-mentioned civil works contracts. During the past years, private local contractors have
demonstrated annual average construction capacities of about US$0.5 million. Joint-ventures with foreign
contractors have an annual capacity of about US$1.0 million; privatized, former State-owned Construction
Enterprises show annual construction capacities of up to US$5.0 million.
3.29  Steel Gates. While it would preferable if contractors were to procure and supply steel gates on
their own, there are serious concerns that such procedures would make it difficult to ensure proper quality
of the steel gates used: (i) local small producers of these gates would likely use scrap metal resulting in
much reduced gate life; and (ii) precise gate dimensions that are required to guarantee optimal fit and to
enable accurate flow control would not be consistently achievable if small unexperienced producers were
to supply these gates. Therefore, to ensure standardization of steel gates for compatibility with existing
equipment; to make use of economies of scale; and to ensure consistently high quality of raw materials
used and gates produced, steel gates would be procured through bulk procurement by the PMU from
selected qualified suppliers, and subsequently be sold to contractors at cost-covering prices. Under ICB
procedures, IDA would finance an aggregate contract volume of US$1.5 million for about three contracts.
3.30  Pumping Equipment. Procurement of pumping equipment would generally involve large contracts.
These would be supply and erect contracts which would include the proper installation of imported pumping
equipment by the supplier. IDA would finance two contract packages at a combined value of US$1.0
million equivalent, to be procured through ICB, using standard bank bidding documents. The remaining
cost of pumping equipment of US$9.1 million would be financed by other donors.
3.31  Other Equipment. All other equipment (total cost of US$2.5 million for maintenance machinery,
laboratory equipment, vehicles, and office equipment) would be financed by IDA. Small equipment
contracts not exceeding an individual volume of US$25,000 (expected total volume of US$0.1 million)
would be procured under Local Shopping procedures based on comparison of price quotations obtained
from at least three local suppliers. Equipment contracts above US$25,000 but not exceeding US$250,000
(expected total volume: US$0.9 million) would be procured through International Shopping based on
comparison of price quotations obtained from at least three eligible suppliers from three different countries.
Equipment contracts above US$250,000 (expected number of contracts: three, expected total volume:
US$1.5 million) would be procured through ICB, using IDA standard bidding documents.
3.32  Technical Assistance. Annex 5. 1 provides detailed information on the technical assistance program
to be financed under the project (total cost of US$3.4 million). IDA would finance US$2.0 million of
technical assistance and training, which would cover consultancies for institutional development (system
O&M: US$0.6 million; design capacity: US$0.2 million, WUA establishment: US$0.5 million; on-farm



- 29 -
water management: US$ 0.2 million) and for implementation support (US$0.5 million). Draft terms of
reference for these assignments have been prepared. Procurement of technical assistance contracts would
follow standard IDA Guidelines for procurement of consultant services. All IDA-financed contracts would
be procured on the basis of short-lists of firms.

3.33   Contract Review and Local Preference. Knowledge of IDA procurement procedures in Albania
is limited. The staff of the PMU would be trained in these procedures (see para below). Meanwhile, in
order to ensure compliance with IDA's Procurement Guidelines, procurement documentation (i.e.,
advertisement, bidding documents, evaluation reports, etc.) for all ICB contracts would be subject to prior
review by IDA. Five civil works contracts to be awarded through LCB, up to US$250,000 equivalent per
contract, would also be subject to IDA's prior review. Similarly, terms of reference for all consultant
assignments, plus other procurement documentation in the case of consultant assignments above US$50,000
contract value, would be subject to prior review by IDA. A detailed list of IDA-financed civil works
contracts has been agreed on during negotiations. Local suppliers competing for contracts under ICB
procedures would receive a preference in bid evaluation of 15 percent of the CIF price, or the prevailing
customs duties, whichever is less, provided it can be proven that the value added to the product in the
country exceeds 20 percent of the ex-factory bid price.
3.34   Procurement Implementation.  All procurement-related activities will be carried out by two
trained local staff of PMU  within MoAF.2' PMU  will receive a total of 18 man/months of technical
assistance and training through an expatriate procurement expert, initially on a full-time basis for twelve
months, then on a part-time basis for the second year of project implementation. This assistance has been
initiated under the PPF. Two training sessions abroad for local staff on procurement of civil works and
equipment would also be financed under the project (see Annex 5. 1). The implementation plan for the
project, including a detailed procurement schedule, is provided in Annex 7.
F. Disbursements
3.35   Disbursement Schedule.  The proposed IDA Credit of US$10.0 million equivalent would be
disbursed over seven fiscal years (see the disbursement schedule in Annex 6.3). The disbursement period
is slightly shorter than the standard disbursement profile for all IDA-financed irrigation and drainage
projects because works under the proposed project would be of a straightforward technical nature, would
mostly deal with simple rehabilitation of existing systems, and would be carried out simultaneously in
various districts and regions by a variety of independent small private local contractors.  IDA
disbursements would be based on full documentation of expenditures only for individual contracts equal
or greater than US$100,000 equivalent. For all other contracts of lesser value, disbursements would be
made on the basis of Statements of Expenditures (SOEs) with the supporting documentation to be retained
by the PMU and made available for review by IDA supervision missions and the auditors. IDA will
reimburse a constant 80 percent share of the expenditures made by PMU against eligible civil works
contracts according to the Lek/US$ parity prevailing at the time such expenditures were made by PMU.22
Proposed disbursement categories for the IDA Credit are shown in table 3.5. The proposed closing date
of the IDA Credit would be June 30, 2001. Donor-provided funds would be disbursed in conformity with
the policies of the donor institutions.
3.36   Special Account. To expedite the execution of the project, a Special Account would be established
in US dollars at a bank acceptable to IDA. The authorized allocation to the Special Account would be
21/    Two PMU staff members have received such training in October 1993 by attending courses on (a) procurement of equipment
(four weeks in Turin, Italy, organized by ILO), and (b) procurement of scattered, low-value civil works (one week in Tirana,
organized by IDA).

US$750,000 (7.5 percent of the IDA Credit) which represents the estimated maximum requirement for a
four-month period. The Special Account would be used to make both foreign and local currency payments;
amounts withdrawn would be converted to the currency of the expenditure at the exchange rate in effect
on the date of payment. The Special Account would be replenished on a monthly basis upon submission
of withdrawal applications supported through appropriate documentation as outlined in para 3.35. In
addition, reconciled bank statements of the Special Account should accompany each replenishment request.
The minimum application size for payments directly from the Credit Account or for issuance of special
commitments is US$70,000.

CHAPTER IV. PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION
A. Implementation Schedule
4.1   Status of Project Preparation. Comprehensive system inventories and detailed, itemized cost
estimates for all works and required pumping equipment in the project districts have been finalized.
Technical designs, including bills of quantities, and bidding documents for rehabilitation works and
pumping equipment are currently being drafted under financing of the Project Preparation Facility.
Technical designs and tender documents for contracts to be awarded during the first year of project
implementation have been presented during negotiations.
4.2   Phasing of Project Components. Annex 10.1 provides an overview of the planned phasing of all
project components. Under funding from Government, from private farmers and from proceeds of the IDA
Credit, system rehabilitation works in the first year of project implementation would start in the three
districts of Kavaja, Lushnje and Fier. These three start-up areas have been selected in light of the
advanced stage of preparation for WUA establishment in these districts. Priority would be given to the
rehabilitation of gravity irrigation and drainage schemes in these districts since these would yield the
highest immediate economic rates of returns. Rehabilitation of the remaining pumping schemes in these
three districts and of all project schemes in the other four project districts would begin by year two (mid-
Q';i      1 vdi c-fillanc:iuliwi K ave been secured. The demonstrationi scheimie for canal flow control



- 32 -
would be designed starting in the first project year, and be built over a 15-month period starting in late
1995 after required irrigation rehabilitation works on the selected scheme and concerned sub-sectors would
have been carried out. Institutional development support would be provided between 1994 and 1996 to
improve system operation and maintenance by WEs. Strengthening of the local design capacity would
focus on the first two years of project implementation, beginning in early 1994 through financing under
the PPF. Support for WUA establishment would be a six-year long-term program, starting in early 1994
through financing under the PPF. The On-Farm Water Management program would be implemented over
a four-year period 1994 to 1997. Studies for policy support would be carried out in 1994 and 1995.
Support for project preparation and implementation would be concentrated on aie first two years, starting
in early 1994 with financing under the PPF.
4.3    Annual Work Plans and Mid-term Review. During negotiations, assurances have been obtained
from Government that detailed Annual Work Plans (AWPs) for all rehabilitation works would be elaborated
and sent to IDA for prior approval, by November 15 of the preceding year, starting with the work program
for calendar year 1995 to be submitted by November 15, 1994. AWPs would be based on the indicative
work program and procurement packages developed during appraisal. AWPs would be prepared by the
PMU, in collaboration with ISDWW and WEs. A joint panel of senior local arid qualified expatriate
experts would be responsible for reviewing AWPs before their transmission to IDA.  Furthermore,
assurances have been obtained from Governinent duriiig negotiations that a comnprehlenisive Mid-Term
Review would be held, by December 31, 1996, to review works completed during the first two project
years and to assess the indicative work program for subsequent project years.
B. Organization and Management
4.4    Project Management. Under the supervision of the Minister of Agriculture and Food, the PMU
within MoAF would be responsible for (i) supervising the preparation of technical designs and tender
documents by ISDWW and WEs for all civil works contracts; (ii) preparation of technical specifications
and bidding documents for equipment and services financed under the project; (iii) procuring all civil
works, goods and services; (iv) coordinating and supervising the iniplemenitation of the project by private
contractors, ISDWW, WEs, WUA Units, the Extension Service, the Soil Institute, and expatriate advisors;
(v) keeping all financial accounts and records; (vi) monitoring and evaluating of project progress and
impact; (vii) developing Annual Work Plans (para 4.3); and (viii) coordinating and communicating with
IDA and other donors. PMU would be staffed by a Director, nominated by the Minister of Agriculture
and Food, and other local staff, including an irrigation engineer, a technical assistance coordinator, two
specialists responsible for procurement, disbursements and accounting, and two staff assistants. PMU staff
would be assisted by expatriate experts in the areas of technical designs and tender documents for civil
works, procurement, disbursements, financial controlling, review of Annual Works Plans, and project
impact assessment. After completion of the project, the PMU would be integrated into a new irrigation
department within MoAF.
4.5   System Rehabilitation Works. Annex 10.2 provides an overview of the process for design,
procurement, construction and supervision of rehabilitation works under the project. ISDWW and WEs
would split the responsibility for designs, development of draft bidding documents, and for supervision of
civil works which would be carried out by private contractors.  To ensure consistency in designs;
standardization of norms; and correctness of technical data, all designs and draft bidding documents
developed by WEs would be routed through ISDWW for review, prior to submission to the PMU which
would assemble the final tender documents. Technical designs and records for all schemes and contracts
would be centrally filed within ISDWW (see organizational chart of ISDWW in Annex 10.3). All contracts



- 33 -
for civil works, equipment and consultant services will be procured, negotiated and signed for by the PMU.
In order to enable small local contractors to finance required construction equipment, PMU will make
initial advance payments to contractors which will be limited to 10 percent of the contract volume if no bid
bonds or performance guarantees can be provided. Subsequent progress payments will be subject to a
corresponding retention clause reflecting the amount of the advance payment.  Contractors that are
considered disqualified due to non-contractual behavior will not be eligible to participate in bidding for
works under the project for at least one subsequent year. The demonstration scheme for canal flow control
would be designed by ISDWW and be built by local contractors, under the supervision by the PMU and
with assistance for design, implementation and training from an expatriate expert.
4.6   Institutional Development Program. To implement the institutional development program under
the project, WEs would be responsible for (i) improving system operation and maintenance in the project
area, including improved cost accounting and financial controlling, after provision of maintenance
machinery, technical assistance and training under the project; and (ii) establishing WUA Units, to be
staffed jointly with staff of the Extension Service, in order to promote the creation of WUAs in the project
area (see organizational chart of WEs in Annex 10.4). ISDWW would be responsible for establishing a
Water Management Unit to coordinate the On-Farm Water Management Program under the project, and
to monitor WE performance in off-farm water management. The Soil Institute would be responsible, under
the On-Farm Water Management Program, for scientific research on its two field stations in Tirana and
Lushnje, and for measuring water and soil salinity in all districts of the project area. The Extension Service
under the District Departments of Agriculture would be responsible for (i) staffing, jointly with WEs,
district-based WUA Units for WUA promotion; and (ii) under the On-Farm Water Management Program,
organizing field demonstrations on improved irrigation practices and on the safe application of pesticides.
C. Supervision, Reporting and Monitoring
4.7   Supervision. The supervision plan in Annex 6.4 provides details on the planned supervision inputs
for the project. After a project launching mission in September 1994, supervision missions during FY95
would ensure that procurement of works, equipment and services be carried out in a timely manner.
Subsequently, two IDA supervision missions would be performed in March and September of each
following calendar year. The March mission would evaluate the status of civil works completed during
the past calendar year and would ensure the successful launching of works for the current year, while the
September mission would discuss and evaluate the AWP for the next fiscal year.
4.8    Reporting and Monitoring. PMU would prepare semi-annual progress reports, copies would be
sent to IDA for review. Reports would include (see key indicators in Annex 7): (i) comprehensive
information on rehabilitation works (timetable of civil works planned, works and pumping equipment
procured, completion rate of ongoing works, works quality, payments made to contractors); (ii) progress
reports on the institutional development and policy support components (equipment and consultant services
procured, copies of consultant reports and studies, summary information on the activities of WEs, WUA
Units, ISDWW, the Soil Institute, all expatriate experts, and on the progress of training programs). PMU
would also prepare and forward to IDA quarterly statements on all financial and procurement records (see
para 4.10 below).
4.9   Impact Assessment. Under assistance from expatriate experts, PMU would prepare semi-annual
project impact assessments, to be included in the progress reports, for review by IDA. Reports would
include: (i) physical indicators, such as number and area coverage of schemes rehabilitated, and the number
and area coverage of WUAs established with number of participating farmers; (ii) development indicators,



- 34 -
such as incremental crop yields and agricultural output in the project area, and incremental farm income;
and (iii) financial indicators related to water pricing, cost recovery achieved, and aggregate budgetary
impact (see Annex 7.3 for project monitoring indicators).
D. Accounts and Audit
4.10   Accounts. PMU would keep all financial accounts and records for the project, comprising (i) a
record of withdrawals on the IDA credit with copies of all disbursement requests and underlying
documentation; and (ii) a record of transactions on the Special Account and copies of the bank statements
of this Account. These records would be updated weekly, and quarterly statements of transactions and
balances, together with updated procurement contract information, would be forwarded to IDA.
4.11   Audit. As a basis for setting water charges, assurances have been obtained from Government
during negotiations that the operations and financial accounts of WEs located in the project area will be
audited on an annual basis by independent, external auditors, with terms of reference acceptable to IDA.
Audits would include the verification of specific performance monitoring indicators for WEs, to be
developed by MoAF, including, inter alia: detailed breakdown of WE costs for system operation and
maintenance (salaries, energy costs, machinery operating costs, depreciation, other costs), operating cost
per ha of command area, staff employed per ha of command area, water charges billed and collected,
percentage deviation of these indicators from the average for all WEs in the project area. Audit results
would subsequently be published locally to ensure the full accountability of WEs towards water users in
each project district. Furthermore, assurances have been obtained from Government at negotiations that
independent audits of project accounts, including audits of SOEs and the Special Account, to be performed
by auditors and under terms of reference acceptable IDA, would be submitted to IDA within 6 months of
the close of each Government fiscal year.
CHAPTER V. PROJECT IMPACT
A. Agricultural Production
5.1    Yields and Cropping Intensities. The project would increase the amount of irrigation water
available at the crop by an average of 400 m3/ha, in order to reach a total of 1,600 m3/ha, for an aggregate
command area of 45,019 ha of at present under-irrigated land. In addition, 28,467 ha of currently rainfed
areas would return to irrigation at 1,600 m3/ha following rehabilitation works under the project. The
project would also improve drainage on a total area of 99,304 ha. Improved irrigation and drainage would
result in higher crop yields, as shown in Annex 1.4. However, due to the poor current cash position of
farmers, use of fertilizer and pesticides will remain limited over a number of years. Therefore, crop yields
are expected to remain below maximum achievable levels, as shown in table 5.1 for major crops in the
project area, until all required farm inputs would be used in appropriate quantities by the fifth year of
project implementation.23  Furthermore, improved irrigation would also allow  for higher cropping
intensities through introduction of double cropping, causing an expected increase of present average
cropping intensities of 90 percent in the project area to about 150 percent.
23/   It is assumed that fertilizer and pesticide use would equal 20 percent of full requirements in year 1, increasing to 100
percent by year 5.



- 35 -
5.2    Cropping Patterns. The seven districts in the project area can be divided into two agro-climatic
zones (see Annex 1.3): zone 1, the Central Coastal Plains, including the districts of Durres, Kavaja,
Lushnje and Fier, with altitudes ranging from sea level up to 180m and a mediterranean climate with dry
warm summers in which all summer crops need irrigation; and zone 2, the Northern Coastal Area, with
the districts of Laci, Kruje and Tirana where altitudes exceed 1,000m and rainfall increases towards the
north, providing for better rainfed conditions for winter wheat, sunflowers and beans, but with a summer
cropping pattern that is similar to zone 1. In both zones, cropping patterns are expected to change due to
improved irrigation and drainage in order to include

However, marketing of produce is at present significantly constrained due to consumers' low domestic
purchasing power, lacking marketing and distribution infrastructure, and limited access to export markets.
Therefore, in the financial and economic analysis of the project, assumptions have been made such as to
reduce the amount of produced and marketed output during the first three years of project implementation
(see Annex 8). Predictions for a potential shift in cropping patterns due to restricted marketing options
can only be made with high uncertainty and have been excluded from the analysis.
B. Farm Income and Poverty Reduction
5.4    Farm Models. For each of the two agro-climatic zones covering the project area (para 5.2), two
farm production models have been developed, reflecting a shift from either rainfed or under-irrigated
conditions to an improved irrigation level of 1,600 m3/ha. Most incremental production would initially be
for self-consumption, with the assumed share of marketed surplus production rising from 25 percent in year
one of the project to 100 percent by year four. Current local price levels of farm inputs and outputs are
assumed to reach world market levels over a five-year period (see Annex 8, Attachment 2). Farmers are
expected to use mostly family labor to cultivate and harvest crops, but they would continue to hire outside
mechanization services for plowing and tilling. It is assumed that credit for seasonal inputs and on-farm
system repairs would be available from either the Rural Commercial Bank, Village Credit Funds, or from
relatives, neighbors and friends to finance 25 percent of operating costs and on-farm investments.
5.5   Impact on Rural Poverty. Table 5.2 provides a summary of the four farm budgets used in the
financial analysis of the project (see Annex 8, Attachment 1). Under the above assumptions on yields,
fertilizer usage and cropping patterns, farmers are expected to have negative net cash-flows, after on-farm



- 36 -
consumption, during the first two years of the project, caused by the assumed initial constraints for
marketing of surplus production. Farmers' average annual net cash income would rise above US$100
equivalent (Lek 10,000) by year three, would exceed US$500 equivalent by year five and would reach
about US$600 at full development of the project. This compares favorably to farm incomes without the
project of about US$200 by year five and no more than US$300 at full development. Due to the phasing
of works, only some 2,300 farm families in the project area would be expected to start benefitting from
investments under the project in the second year of implementation, rising to some 56,800 families at the
end of project implementation, or 45 percent of the total of 126,000 families in the project area.
Additional beneficiaries of the project would include providers of farm services and any incremental hired
farm labor. The project would achieve a significant reduction in overall poverty in the districts concerned
since all beneficiaries would be located within rural areas where the average per-capita income is expected
to remain below income levels of urban areas.

5.6    Role of Women. Women play a prominent part in Albania's irrigated agriculture and are primarily
involved in seeding, harvesting and marketing of crops. The project would, in a limited manner, support
a more active participation of women in irrigation management through women involvement in WUA
activities. WUA training for farmers would focus on upgrading the skills of women as well. The
evaluation of training and workshops under the project would include information on the number of
participants, disaggregated by gender. Similarly, data collection and analysis for impact assessment would
separate between women and men with respect to formal education, wage levels and other income. A
socio-economic study is required to further investigate the present role of women in irrigated agriculture
and to assess their potential role. The study could formulate ways and means to promote a more active
and effective participation of women in all aspects of irrigation management within WUAs. During project
irnplementation, Government should explore sources of grant funding for such a study.
C. Irrigation Management and Cost Recovery
5.7    Cost of Irrigation and Drainage. The true cost of operating, maintaining, and investing into
Albania's irrigation and drainage infrastructure is difficult to assess given the complete lack of any reliable
accounting data at the WE level. In view of the significant share of labor required for operating non-



- 37 -
automated gates and for maintaining a highly depreciated infrastructure, the current annual cost for
operation and maintenance of primary and secondary canals is estimated to range between US$24 and 43
per ha of irrigated and drained command area, depending on the amount of pumping involved and
electricity required in each district, with an average cost of US$32 per ha (see Annex 8.2). Over the
implementation period of the project, these costs are estimated to rise to an average of US$51 per ha by
year 6 of the project, reflecting mainly the expected increase in labor costs and energy prices.24 The total
cost of investments under the project, including design and engineering fees and required subsequent
replacement investmeints, translate into a per-ha charge of about US$15 per year, assuming that farmers
in the project area would be granted a five-year grace period with subsequent repayments over 35 years,
reflecting the conditionis of the IDA Credit. Thlerefore, under full cost recovery, required annual revenues
from water charges, payable by farmers to WEs in the project area, would need to average US$32 per ha
during 1994, rising to about US$66 per ha by 1999 (or about 10 to 15 percent of net farm cash-flow at full
development of the project). The cost of operation and maintenance of systems below the secondary level
by farmers is estimated to equal no more than US$10 per year and ha and would be recovered through
WUA fees. Any on-farn investments into the infrastructure below the secondary canal level would also
be financed by individual farmers or WUAs.
5.8    Cost Recovery Policy. Net caslh-flows for private smallholders in the project area, after on-farm
consumption of outputs, would only become positive by year three of the project (para 5.5). Assuming
a required annual minimilumii cash income of US$100 equivalent per farmer, farmers would only be able to
pay fully cost-covering water charges, and additional WUA fees, after year four of the project. Therefore,
a cost recovery policy would be adopted providing for the gradual increase of water charges to cost-
covering levels over a five-year period. Given the currently irrigated area, and assuming a collection rate
of 60 percent of billed water charges, the present charge level of Lek 1.8/m3 translates into an annual
average revenue of US$13 equivalent per ha (see table 5.3). This represents a coverage level of 41 percent
of the current estimated costs for system operation and maintenance of US$32 equivalent per ha. Under
the project, this percentage would be increased by 15 percent per year, through annual revisions of water
charges, to achieve the full coverage of operation and maintenance costs borne by WEs in the project area
by year five of the project, i.e, by 1998. In addition, starting in 1999, water charges would be increased
further to reflect the full recovery of Government-financed project investments and required replacement
costs. Under the Mid-Term Review, the experience on cost recovery would be reviewed to determine
whether it would be appropriate to extend this cost recovery policy to non-project areas.
5.9    Water Charges. The Irrigation Code would provide the regulatory framework for WE operations,
including principles for water pricing based on actual district-based costs of system operation and
maintenance borne by WEs, as verified by independent external audits (para 4.11). Under the above
assumptions for per-ha costs borne by WEs and investment costs, and assuming a gradual rise in the
collection rate for water charges to 80 percent by year five of the project, cost-covering water charges
would need to rise from their present level of Lek 1 .8/m3 to an average of Lek 4.8/m3 by 1999. This takes
into account the conservative assumption made under the project of a comparatively low consumption rate
after system rehabilitation (1,600 m3/ha/year, see table 5.3). Assuming that a higher average consumption
rate (2,500 m3/ha/year) would be achievable, water charges would need to rise to no more than Lek 3.1/nm3
to be fully cost-covering. An increase in the collection rate for water charges would further reduce the
required rise in the price of irrigation water, to an average charge level of Lek 2.3/m3 by 1999 under an
assumed collection rate of 100 percent (see Annex 8.2). During negotiations, assurances have been
obtained from Government that water charges will be reviewed and adjusted annually, by March 31 of the
24/    Electricity prices are assumed to rise to ahout US$ 0.05/kWh to reach cost-covering levels.



- 38 -
following year, to achieve the full coverage of actual operating costs of WEs in the project area by 1998,
and to recover the full cost of investments under the project and of associated replacements starting in
1999. Water charges would be assessed in March of each year on the basis of the anticipated O&M costs
for the coming irrigation season, final adjustments would be made at the end of October of each year to
reflect actual O&M costs incurred by WEs.

5.10    Budgetary Impact.  As set out in table 5.3, budgetary subsidies to WEs in the project area are
estimated to equal about US$1.6 and 1.7 million equivalent in 1994 and 1995, and would fall to US$1.3
and 0.8 million in 1996 and 1997, respectively, in order to be phased out by 1998.25 Assurances have been
obtained from Government during negotiations that annual budget provisions for WEs, over the
implementation period of the project, will include sufficient funds to cover the portion of recurrent costs
for system operation and maintenance not recovered through water charges.
5.11    Organizing Water Delivery and Payments. Water requests and charges would be collected from
farmers by WUA officials who would forward consolidated water requests and payments to WEs. Water
would be delivered by WE technicians in the field to the tertiary canal level, where WUAs would take over
responsibility for subsequent water scheduling and distribution among WUA members (see Annex 10.5).
25/     These subsidy figures compare to current subsidies of US$ 2.2 million equivalent in 1993 for all WEs in the country.
Estimated subsidies for 1994 through 1997 include an aggregate amount of US$ 1.2 million to account for incremental
recurrent costs incurred by WEs in the project area due to improved and additional system operation and maintenance under
the project (para 3.13).

D. Environmental Impact
5.12   Agricultural Runoff and Soil Salinity.  The overall impact of the rehabilitation works and
institutional and policy reforms on the natural environment would be positive. The project would reduce
surface water and soil contamination stemming from agricultural runoff through a reduction in irrigation
water losses by improving system conveyance and flow control. Pesticide run-off would be further reduced
through dissemination of information to farmers on the safe use of pesticides, following IDA regulations
on techniques for integrated pest management, in conjunction with the On-farm Water Management
Program supported under the project. Improvements in system conveyance and on-farm irrigation water
distribution would also diminish the potential for soil salinity built up through water-logging caused by
water losses, mainly in coastal zones. Rehabilitation of major drainage systems along the coastal districts
would further improve flood control and reduce salinity-related problems without threatening valuable
natural wetlands or habitats since all areas were drained previously.
5.13   Environmental Evaluation. The project would improve the country's capacity of monitoring soil
and surface water quality through provision of equipment and technical assistance to the Soil Institute in
Tirana. However, given the potentially serious water quality problems in the country, additional assurances
have been obtained from Government at negotiations that an environmental evaluation would be prepared,
and be submitted for review by IDA, for each irrigation and drainage scheme prior to inviting the first
works contract bid for each respective scheme.26  This evaluation would address, inter alia, (i)
contamination of irrigation water upstream caused by domestic and industrial wastes; (ii) pollution
downstream caused by drainage water discharge; and (iii) proposed measures to mitigate any such
environmental impacts. The evaluation would be part of sub-project design and would be carried out by
ISDWW. The Soil Institute would be consulted for comments on each evaluation before submission for
review by IDA. In addition, the overall environmental impact of the project would be assessed during the
Mid-Term Review.
5.14   Other Environmental Impacts.  Institutional and policy reforms under the project would result
in improved cost recovery, allowing for adequate maintenance of reservoirs and irrigation and drainage
systems, thus ensuring flood control measures, perennial river runoff, and the safeguarding of fish and
aquatic life forms. Rehabilitation of the irrigation infrastructure would also reduce the need for off-farm
grazing in the hills and mountains which would reduce erosion and facilitate rejuvenation of forest lands.
Through creation of additional employment and income opportunities in rural areas, the project would
reduce incentives for migrating to urban areas and would contribute to the long-term objective of sustained
land use. The project has been reviewed and placed in environmental category 'B' consistent with the
provisions of OD 4.01.
5.15   Water Resources Management. While Albania has not yet developed a comprehensive water
resources development and management plan, preparatory steps have been taken by Government to develop
such a plan, and IDA staff is currently involved in mobilizing support for this effort. The proposed project
would fit well into a new water resources management plan since this plan would include substantial
investments for rehabilitating water development works. Furthermore, there are currently no water
allocation issues between economic sectors since water resources exceed by far current water requirements
(para 1.7), and seasonal variations can be balanced through the existing reservoir storage capacity. In
addition, IDA and other donors are involved in several water supply and sanitation projects which would
26/    A first environmental screening has been undertaken by ISDWW and WEs during project preparation in order to exclude
schemes with any serious water quality issues from rehabilitation under the proposed project.



- 40 -
initiate a systematic municipal sanitation program within the framework of an overall water resources
development and management plan.
E. Economic Analysis
5.16   Assumptions. Over a twenty year period, both incremental recurrent and replacement costs have
been added to investment costs under the project to reflect the full economic costs of the project. Import
and export parity prices have been calculated for tradeable inputs and outputs, non-traded goods have been
priced at domestic financial prices. Unskilled labor has been valued at a low opportunity cost while skilled
labor for construction works has been valued at prevailing financial prices. In accordance with the project
implementation schedule, phasing of construction works has been assumed such that 4 percent of the works
would be completed in 1995, followed by cumulative completion rates of 27, 55, 80 and 100 percent for
years 1996-1999. Incremental farm production was estimated assuming that each farm in the project area
would achieve maximum projected yields and incremental outputs with a five-year time-lag after completion
of rehabilitation works in order to allow for a temporarily limited use of fertilizer and pesticides. Current
marketing constraints for agricultural produce have been taken into consideration by reducing the economic
value of farm production by 75, 50, and 25 percent during the first three years of project implementation
since farmers are expected to decrease production of
non-marketable surplus output, after satisfying their

rise by 35 percent, or project benefits drop by 25
percent compared to appraisal values to make project investments non-viable. An increase in construction
costs of more than 35 percent is highly unlikely given the advanced stage of project design. A reduction
of benefits by more than 25 percent is equally unlikely in light of the already conservative assumptions
made about farmers' ability to market incremental surplus production. The project would also remain
economically viable under the assumption of a two-year time-lag for expected benefits to materialize.
F. Project Risks
5.18   Delays in Implementation. The principal project risks stem from the limited implementation
capacity of the various Government and private-sector institutions involved in the implementation of the
project, potentially causing delays in design, procurement and delivery of rehabilitation works. The
comprehensive technical assistance and training program for all public-sector institutions involved in the
project would help reduce these risks. Delays caused by a potential lack of qualified local contractors to



- 41 -
carry out works under the project would be addressed by inviting experienced contractors from neighboring
countries to participate in the local competitive bidding process. Furthermore, as demonstrated by the
sensitivity analysis, the project would remain economically viable even if rehabilitation works and resulting
project benefits were delayed by up to two years. The risk of delays in implementing the institutional
development sub-components are limited given the advanced stage of implementation preparation through
provision of technical assistance and training to the Project Management Unit, financed under the PPF.
A delay in the formation of WUAs is unlikely given the strong incentives for their creation introduced
under the project, i.e. WUA establishment will be a precondition for any repair works at the tertiary level,
and Water Enterprises will only deliver water to established WUAs, not to individual farmers.
5.19   Mobilization of Cofinancing. Despite the strong interest shown by a number of cofinanciers in
the project and corresponding preliminary commitments obtained (see para 3.25), there is a risk that not
all required funding from donors would be secured in time. However, given the fully modular structure
of the project with rehabilitation works to be carried independently from each other in seven districts, the
project would still remain economic in the event that selected schemes in one or several districts could not
be rehabilitated as scheduled due to lack of cofinancing. Even in the hypothetical case that only IDA-
financed rehabilitation works in the three districts of Kavaje, Lushnje and Fier would be carried out, the
average economic rate of return would equal at least 17 percent since IDA-financed schemes are for the
most part high-return gravity schemes, and since financing of all required institutional development and
project implementation support would be secured by IDA.
5.20   Other Risks. The potential lack of funds to cover local costs of 37 percent of total project costs
is a further project risk but would be limited since 80 percent of local costs would be financed through
participation of IDA and other cofinanciers. Nevertheless, the risk remains that a deterioration of the
Government's overall fiscal balance could threaten the provision of sufficient budgetary funds to WEs
during the transitional period until the full recovery of recurrent and investment costs through water
charges would be achieved. During the mid-term review, a broad assessment of project implementation
and financing would be undertaken with the view of addressing and resolving these financial risks. In
addition, the project's monitoring mechanisms are expected to reduce risks in general by identifying
potential problems at an early stage, including those associated with institutional development and
financing. Also, during project supervision, particular emphasis will be given to ensure close coordination
between the various donors towards the consistent application of cost recovery policies agreed to under the
proposed project. A continued lack of marketing opportunities for farmers of their surplus production is
a further risk. This has been taken into consideration in the financial and economic analysis by making
highly conservative assumptions regarding the potential sale of incremental production by project farmers.
Even with these assumptions, the sensitivity analysis reveals that a reduction in farm benefits of up to 25
percent could be sustained without making the project non-viable.
CHAPTER VI. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATION
6.1    During negotiations, assurances have been obtained that the Government would:
(a) submit detailed Annual Work Plans (AWP) for all rehabilitation works, by November 15 of the
preceding year, starting with the work program for calendar year 1995 (para 4.3);
(b) hold a comprehensive Mid-Term Review by December 31, 1996 (para 4.3);



- 42 -
(c) cover all applicable duties and local taxes under the project (para 3.23);
(d) provide funding to cover any financing gap in the event that sufficient cofinancing could not
be secured by October 31, 1995 (para 3.25);
(e) ensure that the guidelines for comparative price quotations in the award of civil works contracts
of lesser value than US$250,000, as set out in para 3.27, would be followed;
(f) undertake annual independent audits of Water Enterprises in the project area for rehabilitation
works, with terms of reference acceptable to IDA (para 4. 11);
(g) review and adjust water charges annually, by March 31 of the following year, to achieve full
coverage of actual operating costs of WEs in the project area by 1998, and to recover the full
cost of investments under the project and of associated replacements starting in 1999 (para 5.9);
(h) ensure that annual budget provisions for WEs, over the implementation period of the project,
include sufficient funds to cover the portion of recurrent costs for system operation and
maintenance not recovered through water charges (para 5.10);
(i) ensure that an environmental evaluation would be prepared, and be submitted for review by
IDA, for each irrigation and drainage scheme prior to inviting the first works contract bid for
each respective scheme (para 5.13);
(j) submit to IDA independent audits of project accounts, including audits of SOEs and the Special
Account, to be performed by auditors and under terms of reference acceptable to IDA, within
6 months of the close of each Government fiscal year (para 4. 11).
6.2    The following conditions of Board presentation have been fulfilled:
(a) the Irrigation Code has been passed by Parliament (para 3.17);
(b) arrears in energy charges that have been billed to the seven WEs in the project area by KESH,
the Albanian power utility, have been cleared (para 3.13).
6.3    Enactment of WUA Regulations through a decision by the Minister of Agriculture and Food would
be a condition of effectiveness of the IDA Credit (para 3.17).
6.4    With the above agreements and conditions, the proposed Project would be suitable for a Credit of
SDR7.1 million (US$10 million equivalent) at standard terms with 40 years maturity.

(111) POLICY SUPPORT
1.   Regulatory              Study: Regulatory Framework for Irrization                                                       Jan. - June 1994      60,000
Framework for           Assist the Ministry of Agriculture and Food in the finalization of the Irrigation Code as the required  3 rn/m @ US$20,000
Irrigation             legal basis for irrigation management. The Irrigation Code will define the role of Water Enterprises,
WUAs and Government and would include additional provisions related, inter alia, to small-system
turnover and right-of-way issues. Assist in finalizing the draft WUA Legislation, to be enacted by the
Ministry of Agriculture and Food as a decree based on the new Irrigation Code.
2.   Small System            Study: Small-System Turnover Program & Timetable                                                 Jan. - June 1995      60,000
Turnover                On the basis of the June 1993 Presidential Decree, develop program and timetable for the transfer of  3 m/m @ US$20,000
ownership and management responsibility for small systems to WUAs within the project area for
rehabilitation works. Take into consideration international experience, and lay out expected effects of
small-system turnover on farmers' productivity and profitability, implications on Water Enterprises'
budgets (if any), and the prospects of financial and economic viability and sustainability of small                             l 
irrigation systems. Address aspects of equity, rural development and the overall social impact of
system turnover.
3.   Water User              Study: Water User Association Consolidation                                                      July - Dec. 1995      60,000
Association (WUA)       Design program and timetable for the consolidation of initially village-based WUAs in the project area    3 m/m @ US$ 20,000
Consolidation          into larger organizations, such as water user boards or WUA unions, in order to more closely reflect
hydraulic units that are served by a single source of water. These new organizations would take over
O&M responsibilities for secondary canals and related flow structures.
4.  Water Charges            Study: Water Charges                                                                             Jan. - June 1995      100,000
Establish a comprehensive data base, and design and assist in implementation of an adequate financial    5 m/m @ US$ 20,000
model for determining cost-covering levels of water charges, based on external audits of the 1994
financial accounts of Water Enterprises in the project area. Explore alternative models for water tariff
differentiation by district and scheme and by cost components for investment and recurrent costs.
Closely collaborate with the foreign expert providing TA on cost accounting to Water Enterprises.
5.   Private Pumping         Study: Private Surface and Groundwater Pumping Development                                       July - Dec. 1995      40,000
Development             Identify legal, institutional and financial constraints in the enabling environment that limit farmers'  2 m/m @ US$ 20,000
access to irrigation water stemming from both private surface and groundwater pumping.
