ANNEX 5.2: Canal Flow Control
1.     Background.  Water distribution in large irrigation schemes is generally constrained by the
rigidity inherent to any large canal system in adjusting water supply to changing demand. Unless carefully
implemented following a long process of trial and error, the operation of large canal systems often results
in sequences of water over-spill and/or shortages until an equilibrium is found. The difficulty increases
with the length and complexity of the canal system. There are four basic prerequisites for a canal system
to overcome these operational constraints and to adequately meet farmers' demand for water: (a) a well
structured canal grid with a clear and logical hierarchy of primary, secondary and tertiary canals; (b) a
consistent flow control system, allowing to rapidly adjust water levels in each canal in response to
variations in water demand and supply, and to measure the quantities of water discharged at each in-take;
(c) adequate operating procedures that serve in establishing a close link between system operators and water
users; and (d) sufficient system maintenance to ensure the reliability of water deliveries.
2.      Current Operating Conditions in Albania. The first of the above-mentioned prerequisites -- an
adequately structured canal system -- is substantially met in all large irrigation systems in Albania, with
a clear and rational network of water supply and drainage canals serving irrigation blocks of about 12 ha
within a geometrical grid pattern. However, in the current situation, the three remaining prerequisites for
efficient canal operation are far from being met in Albania. Although being designed adequately for
manual upstream control, the flow control system is not in good working order following destruction or
deterioration of most canal structures. Furthermore, water distribution by Water Enterprises is inadequate
due to the lack of communication, transport and measuring equipment, insufficiently trained staff and poor
maintenance of the infrastructure.
3.      Proposed Approach. Modern technology can provide for efficient control of water movement
and accurate measurement of water abstraction at all levels of well designed canal systems in Albania.
However, introduction of modern technology would require substantial redesign of canal profiles and
control systems over entire schemes, major modifications of the infrastructure, installation of expensive
control hardware, and in-depth training of operators in the use of technologically sophisticated procedures.
In line with the general project approach, which is quick rehabilitation of infrastructure for the largest
possible area, any preconstruction design activities and capital cost for rehabilitation should be kept at a
reasonable minimum. In the particular case of canal control, this translates into simple technology, focus
on improved operating procedures, and acceptance of below-optimal hydraulic performance. In terms of
efficiency, the project objective is to achieve an overall irrigation efficiency of about 40 percent for both
water distribution and on-farm application. However, to prepare for a more in-depth upgrading of canal
control, to be introduced at a later stage, the project would provide financing for a canal control
demonstration scheme on about 1,000 ha, serving as a training tool for system designers and operators and
as a national experimentation center.
4.      Flow Control Demonstration Scheme. This scheme would cover about 1,000 ha, served by
canals that have previously been rehabilitated under the project. Selected canals would form a single unit,
and the scheme would include a portion of a primary canal. Suitable sites have been identified in the
Lushnje district (main canal of LUU1 scheme, for a length of 10 to 15 km, including the irrigated area
upstream of the canal and on the left bank). The project would finance an automatic upstream control
system, including "duckbill" weirs and baffle distributors on primary and secondary canals. The total
incremental cost of infrastructure investments under the scheme is estimated at US$ 400 per ha, or a total
base cost of US$ 400,000. This amount would cover the reconstruction of all cross-regulators and water
in-takes. The scheme would be designed, under assistance from an expatriate expert, starting in the first



Annex 5                                          - 78 -
Page 8 of 23
project year, and be built over a 15-month period starting in late 1995 after completion of rehabilitation
works on the selected scheme. A total of 8 man/months of technical assistance and related training abroad
would be financed under the project (total base cost of US$ 200,000, see Annex 5.1).
5.      Canal and Flow Control in Rehabilitated Schemes. In addition to the demonstration scheme,
the project would address canal and flow control through system rehabilitation works, and through
provision of equipment and technical assistance for improved system operation and maintenance (see Annex
5.3). With respect to rehabilitation works, the project would aim at increasing the efficiency of water
distribution by reducing water spill-over or discharge to drains, while ensuring flexibility, reliability and
equity in the delivery of water quantities requested by farmers. Specifically, works under the project would
target canal control and flow control in the following manner:
(a) Canal Control. There are four widely used methods to control water movement in canal systems
under variable conditions of upstream demand: upstream control, downstream control, constant
volume control, and controlled volume control. The latter two methods require sophisticated flow
measurement and data transmission techniques and cannot be considered at this stage of irrigation
development in Albania. The downstream control method might be particularly cost-effective on
primary canals and provide for much greater flexibility in supply/demand adjustment. Nevertheless,
it would require expensive heightening of canal banks, in addition to installing costly automatic
cross-regulators, and can, therefore, only be considered for the future. Therefore, the upstream
control method appears as most appropriate for the current conditions in Albania as this method fits
well into the original system design and, if systems are properly rehabilitated, could significantly
increase irrigation efficiency.
Four types of cross-regulators are usually considered for upstream control, namely: manual gates
(initially installed in the Albanian schemes), longitudinal or "duckbill" weirs, automatic float gates,
and automatic motorized gates. The latter would be part of a more sophisticated canal control
approach, including level detectors and electronic controllers, and would not be included under the
project. Longitudinal weirs and automatic float gates are simple and robust options but would
require an expensive and time-consuming remodelling of all existing cross-regulators. Therefore,
using existing manual regulators, after rehabilitation, will be the least expensive option, guaranteeing
the largest area coverage of rehabilitation works under the project. However, given the relatively
low efficiency of manual regulators, upgrading of operational procedures for the staff of Water
Enterprises would be required for the hydraulic efficiency to reach the intended level (see related
technical assistance described in Annex 5.3).
(b) Flow Control. A flow control system must be available at each in-take with a view to accurately
estimate the volume of water transported and finally delivered to water users. Under the project,
this would be achieved through: (a) on primary canals, substitution of the current use of theoretical
and largely inaccurate gate rating curves through periodic gauged rating curves, provided that
adequate gauging equipment and trained technicians are available to Water Enterprises; (b) on
secondary canals, rehabilitation of existing gauging systems, including gates and measuring devices,
which would be more cost effective than the installation of baffle distributors, although the latter
would be technologically more efficient; (c) on tertiary canals, rehabilitation of farm-based flow
control gates, where flow can be easily measured by rectangular weirs or Parshall flumes.



- 79 -                                       Annex 5
Page 9 of 23
6.   Estimated System Efficiency. This proposed canal and flow control system would not allow for
rapid and efficient adjustments to frequently changing water supply and demand. However, the conditions
in Albania suggest that system capacity would need adjusting only during three periods: (a) the peak period
between the end of May and end-August where water demand is at a stable maximum due to the absence
of rain, and due to high evapo-transpiration and limited canal capacity; and (b) rather short periods before
May and after August when flow needs to be adjusted to a reduced demand in spring and late summer.
Under these conditions, the operational efficiency of the system is estimated to reach about 85 percent (95
percent each for primary, secondary and tertiary canals). Under a conveyance efficiency estimated at 80
percent (assuming that water leaks in the system would not exceed 20 percent after rehabilitation under the
project), and given an assumed on-farm water application efficiency of about 60 percent, the overall system
efficiency would average about 40 percent, a reasonable and acceptable performance in the present context.



Annex 5                                       - 80 -
Page 10 of 23
ANNEX 5.3: Improvement of System Operation and Maintenance
1.    Background. Operation and Maintenance (O&M) activities are placed under the responsibility of
district-based state-owned Water Enterprises (WEs).  In the new  smallholder environment, basic
responsibilities of WEs for system O&M cover distribution of irrigation water to the tertiary level, and
drainage of excess water from the farm gate to the sea by a network of canals, pipes and pumping stations.
Small reservoirs exclusively assigned to irrigation are placed under WE responsibility while large
multipurpose dams are operated by hydro-energy producing state utilities. O&M of tertiary irrigation
canals and buried drains is the responsibility of farmers. WEs are also responsible for billing and
collecting water charges from farmers.
2.    Although O&M duties have not changed under the new smallholder regime, the context in which
they are performed has been profoundly modified by the combined effect of (i) the emergence of a
multitude of small individual farmers in place of a limited number of large state and cooperative farms;
(ii) the phasing out of Government subsidies for O&M activities carried out by WEs; (iii) the general
obsolescence of WEs' equipment; and (iv) the massive deterioration of the irrigation and drainage
infrastructure.  As a result, WEs find themselves in a non-sustainable situation where poor water
distribution services by WEs discourage farmers from to pay for water deliveries, thus reducing WEs'
financial resources which, in turn, makes proper O&M impossible.
3.    Rationale for Improving O&M. Improving O&M is of a crucial importance for several reasons:
(i) the public irrigation infrastructure requires adequate and permanent maintenance, in particular following
the rehabilitation effort supported by the project; (ii) the efficiency of water distribution to farmers
requires improvement to offset insufficient system capacities imposed by the initial design; and (iii)
incentives need to be created for farmers to pay for increased and cost-covering water charges.
4.    Project Approach. The project would support WEs as autonomous public enterprises which are
responsible for providing adequate water delivery to farmers and are maintaining the public infrastructure.
WEs would be strengthened by Government through provision of additional O&M equipment and setting
up of appropriate O&M techniques and procedures. Some support from the Government budget for WEs
would be required initially until the full recovery of actual O&M costs through water charges will be
achieved.
5.    Project Approach. The project would provide each WE in the project area with a minimum set
of O&M equipment and materials including an excavator, a cement mixer, workshop equipment, works
and material for the rehabilitation of buildings and workshops, vehicles, office equipment and radio
communication technology. The equipment would complement machinery provided under the FY92
Critical Imports Project. The set of O&M equipment provided under the proposed project represents the
minimum required to initiate O&M improvement, and additional equipment would be required in the future
to strengthen WEs' ability to carry out adequate O&M activities. Such additional equipment would be
financed out of WEs' budgets, and the investment cost would be recovered through water charges.
6.    Provision of O&M Technical Assistance and Training. The project would support, through
technical assistance and staff training, the following selected priority areas with the objective of improving
O&M techniques and procedures:



- 81 -                                         Annex 5
Page I I of 23
(a) Water distribution and canal flow control. In view of the operational rigidity inhei er] to any
large canal system, appropriate control equipment and operational procedures should be intr oduced
to ensure efficiency, flexibility and equity in water deliveries to farmers. Simple and cost-effective
improvements would be introduced in designing the rehabilitation works supported by the project.
In addition, a demonstration scheme of 1000 ha would be constructed using sophisticated modern
control systems. The project would also support technical assistance and training to iiiipiove
operational procedures for water delivery and for water charges billing and collection trIough (i)
evaluation of the current operational procedures, (ii) elaboration of proposals to imipr-ove
operational procedures, (iii) design of forms and manuals for two selected districts and provision
of hands-on assistance at start-up; and (iv) periodic monitoring of WE performance iri water
deliveries.
(b) Maintenance of subsurface drains. In some low-lying coastal areas, the sustainability of irrigated
agriculture is dependent upon the control of waterlogging and soil salinity. Heavy investments
have been made in the past in well structured drainage systems, including sub-surface tile dialis
in the most salinity-prone areas. However, maintenance of sub-surface drains has been inadequate
and their operating efficiency is seriously reduced. Appropriate maintenance techniques, equipment
and procedures should be identified, acquired and adopted to maintain subsurface drailns, and a
performance monitoring mechanism should subsequently be set up. The project would support
related technical assistance.
(c) Maintenance of pumping equipment. Within the project area, there is a large variety of pumping
stations using different types of equipment at various levels of amortization, some of which will
be replaced under the project. Proper maintenance of pumping equipment is of critical itmiportance
for the sustainability of these investments.  Appropriate methods to organize and perform
maintenance of pumps, electric motors and transformers need to be established to iiiiniitor
equipment performance, schedule preventive maintenance activities, maintain stocks of spare parts,
prepare annual maintenance budgets and plan for replacements.  Under the project, mlodern
computer-assisted methods for maintenance organization of pumping equipment would be developed
in two districts (Tirana and Lushnje) with the expectation that these concepts and proceduires wvould
be further disseminated into other districts. Related technical assistance and training would be
financed.
(d) Cost accounting. WEs require an analytical financial cost accounting system to determine and
control actual district-based O&M costs, including depreciation of investments into ma( hinery and
equipment, as a basis for charging cost-covering water fees to water users. Furthermore, detailed
and accurate financial statements will be the basis to carry out a comparative analysis of WEs'
performance and efficiency, and will enable annual external, independent audits to take place. The
project would support related technical assistance and training for all WEs in the project area.
7.    Implementation Arrangements. The responsibility for improving O&M  techniques vwould be
borne by WEs in the project area which will receive the above-mentioned equipment, technical assistance
and training. In addition, a Water Management Unit would be established within the Tirana-based Institute
for Study and Design of Water Works (ISDWW) to monitor WE performance in off-lalm  water
management. The Water Management Unit would also be coordinating the On-Farm Water Management
Program supported under the project. MoAF will monitor the financial performance of WEs through its
Economic Directorate, following annual external audits of WEs' accounts.



Annex 5                                        - 82 -

ANNEX 5.5: Establishment of Water User Associations
Introduction
1.      Prior to the 1991 democratic revolution, the agrarian structure in Albania had an entirely
collective character. Land was farmed by about 500 agricultural cooperatives (ACs) on some 534,000 ha
and by about 150 state farms (SFs) on 151,000 ha. Irrigation is being practiced since early this century,
and land under irrigation was steadily increased until the end of 1990 when it extended to about 424,000
ha. SFs had a higher percentage of their land under irrigation command, and were engaged in more
advanced irrigation practices, than ACs. The dissolution of ACs in 1991 and the following privatization
of SF land resulted in the emergence of some 400,000 small farm holdings between 0.5 and 3.0 ha, with
an average size of 1.4 ha. Also in 1991, communes were established as administrative bodies comprising
on average three to four villages each.
Past Situation
2.    Irrigation and Drainage Management. Some 81 percent of irrigation and drainage systems were
centrally administered by the Land, Water and Rural Development Department (LWRD) of the Ministry
of Agriculture and Food (MoAF) through public Water Enterprises (WEs) at the district level. Irrigation
and drainage systems up to the tertiary canal level were operated and managed by WEs, the construction
of irrigation works was carried out by state-owned Construction Enterprises (CEs). The remaining 19
percent of irrigation and drainage systems on an area of about 78,000 ha was managed by ACs without
involvement of WEs. These systems generally utilized a single, independent source of water such as a
small reservoir or a well. WEs' responsibility for irrigation water delivery and distribution among SFs
and ACs, and for operation and maintenance (O&M) of irrigation and drainage canals, ended at the
secondary outlets (tertiary canals). WEs supplied the water under contractual agreements to ACs and SFs.
Subsequent on-farm distribution of water and maintenance of tertiary and quaternary canals were the
responsibility of each AC or SF, under the supervision by WEs. Under these conditions, individual
farmers were not involved at all irrigation management.
3.    Regulatory Framework. The relationship between ACs as water users and WEs as water suppliers
was codified in several pieces of legislation, primarily: (i) a 1967 Decree and a 1988 Decision of the
Council of Ministers on the construction and administration of irrigation and drainage systems, (ii) the Civil
Code of 1982, and (iii) 1989 Regulations of the Minister of Agriculture and Food on the maintenance and
exploitation of agricultural water works. These regulations addressed the responsibilities of both WEs and
ACs. After the disappearance of ACs, this regulatory framework became obsolete.
Present Situation
4.     Institutional Vacuum. The privatization of agricultural land created the need for institutional
changes in the management and operation of irrigation and drainage systems with respect to water delivery,
water distribution, and system O&M below the secondary canal level. While WEs remained responsible
for water supply, an organizational vacuum was created on the water user side since mechanisms for
coordinating irrigation water scheduling, collecting water charges from individual farmers, and for system
operation and maintenance beyond the secondary outlets were not available. At the same time, CEs were
being set up for privatization, to be completed by March 1994.



- 87 -                                         Annex 5
Page 17 of 23
5.    Need for Water Users Associations (WUAs). At the central level, MoAF remains responsible,
through LWRD, for the overall organization of irrigation and drainage in the country. At the district level,
state-owned WEs are delivering water to individual farmers, rather than ACs and SFs. Since WEs are
unable to communicate and to enter into contracts with the multitude of smallholder farmers, WEs started,
in 1992, requiring farmers to elect village representatives who would collect water requests and payments
of water charges and forward them to WEs. It is envisaged that WUAs will be formed to take over and
expand the role of village representatives, thus ensuring the full and decentralized participation of farmers
in irrigation and drainage management.
6.     Legal Basis for WUAs Establishment.  Under the Bank-financed Critical Imports Project,
Legislation for Water Users Associations has been drafted, including a Model Instrument for the legal
establishment and registration of individual WUAs and related Model Statutes for WUAs. This Legislation
is expected to be enacted through a decision by the Minister of Agriculture and Food, following the passage
of a new Irrigation Code by Parliament. In the interim, a Presidential Decree (No. 576 of June 28, 1993)
has been enacted to provide a legal basis for the turnover of management and ownership of irrigation and
drainage systems -- managed in the past by ACs and serving no more than one commune -- to the local
commune level. Under the Decree, communes are responsible for organizing water distribution by setting
up WUAs, for irrigation infrastructure investments within the system, and for setting water charges through
decision by the commune Council.
7.    Farmer Survey. To assess the prospects for WUA establishment, a survey among 60 farmers has
been conducted in all districts forming the area of rehabilitation works under the proposed project. The
results of the survey (see Attachments 1 and 2) indicate that farmers are clearly willing to join into a
"group-organized" water management as they expect to benefit from a better level of service through more
reliable water supply. Interviewed farmers also expressed the readiness to perform maintenance on tertiary
and quaternary canals themselves. Furthermore, the survey demonstrated farmers general willingness to
pay for irrigation water and WUA membership fees if water is delivered in a reliable manner. Many
farmers appear ready to change their cropping pattern, if required, in order to increase the value of their
marketed production such as to be able to pay for water charges. Farmers expressed the hope that WUAs
could eventually provide additional services related, e.g., to the marketing and distribution of their
production.
8.    First WUAs Created. At present, farmers appear not have specific knowledge about the purpose
and organizational set-up of WUAs. However, in three districts (Lushnje, Fier and Kavaje) farmers
voluntarily formed several WUAs on a village basis. In the case of one WUA established in the Lushnje
district, farmers became familiar with the principles of WUAs through distribution of a copy of the
proposed WUA Legislation. Upon the initiative of the village chief the participation of all farmers in the
respective village (280 farm families, 432 ha of irrigable land) was achieved and a five-member WUA
Council with a chairman was elected. This experience provides evidence that farmers are capable of taking
initiative and making decisions to organize themselves into water user groups.
9.    Required Support for WUA Establishment. The process of establishing WUAs in Albania
requires solid support at the district level from WEs in conjunction with the staff of the Extension Service
under the district Department of Agriculture. WE have a strong interest in farmers organizing themselves
into WUAs in order to facilitate water distribution and collection of charges. Important catalysts for WUA
creation are also the communes where agronomists of the Extension Service are stationed. At the local
level, elected village chiefs who are acting as village representatives for dealing with WEs can favorably



Annex 5                                        - 88 -
Page 18 of 23
influence the progress of WUAs development. Village chiefs can lead the process of institutional
transformation by bringing about a change in farmers' thinking and their understanding of the potential
benefits of WUA  establishment.  The recent emergence of Private Farmers Associations (PFAs)
demonstrates that farmers are interested in mutually beneficial collaborative arrangements, despite their
forced collectivization under the former political regime. Existing PFAs can, therefore, serve as promoting
agents for the establishment of WUAs.
WUA Establishment Under the Critical Imports Project
10.   Selection of WUA Promoters. Under the Irrigation Component of the FY92 Critical Imports
Project, WUA establishment on a pilot basis is under way in the districts forming the area for rehabilitation
works under the proposed project. An expatriate WUA trainer developed an Action Plan which is seeking
the participation of entities at the central, district and local levels. A centralized training and information
seminar in Tirana was held in September 1993 involving directors of WEs and heads of the district
Departments of Agriculture. The aim of the seminar was to foster a joint effort in support of developing
WUAs, as well as to resolve potential differences between WEs and Departmnent of Agriculture staff with
respect to the objective and the proposed approach for WUA creation. In each district, eight WE staff and
extension workers were nominated for subsequent training in order to become future WUA promoters for
WUA establishment, to be based in district-based WUA Units. WUA promoters were selected based on
their general technical competency in irrigation and drainage, familiarity with local agricultural practices,
and good interpersonal and organizational skills.
11.   Training for WUA Promoters. Two training sessions for WUA promoters, of one week duration
for 25 participants each, will be held by end-1993. The training will start with an introduction into aspects
of rural development within the Albanian setting. The importance of WUAs will be highlighted as suitable
vehicles not only for improvements in irrigation water delivery and system O&M, but also for better use
of agricultural inputs, development of best farming techniques, and for marketing and distribution of
outputs. Training sessions will be divided in two parts: (i) an institutional part, including an explanation
of the proposed WUA Legislation, a description of suggested Model statutes and internal regulations for
WUAs, and aspects of financial management and accounting for WUAs; and (ii) a promotional part dealing
with mechanisms for motivating and assisting farmers to establish and register WUAs.
Promotion of WUAs under the Proposed Project
12.   WUAs as Precondition for Rehabilitation and Water Delivery. Over the implementation period
of the project, WUAs will be established in all areas where project rehabilitation works are to be carried
out. In particular, WUA establishment will be a precondition for any rehabilitation works below the
secondary canal level. Furthermore, the new Irrigation Code will require the establishment of WUAs as
a condition for delivery of water to agricultural users. Subsequently, the creation of WUAs would also
include areas not affected by rehabilitation works under the project, such as command areas where former
AC s used to manage irrigation and drainage independently from WEs. Depending on the experience gained
from establishing WUAs in the project area, information on WUAs would be disseminated to all districts
in the country in order to create the required institutional awareness for further rehabilitation works on a
national basis under a potential follow-up project.
13.    Principles for WUA Establishment. WUA would be established based on democratic principles,
through the majority of vote of all concerned farmers. Although hydrological units would be the preferred



- 89 -                                       Annex 5
Page 19 of 23
physical basis for WUA establishment, WUA boundaries would respect strong, long-established ties among
and between farm families and within villages. The area coverage of individual WUAs would range
between 50 and 400 ha, with an average membership of about 200 farmers, a minimum initial membership
of 15 farmers, and a maximum number of 2,000 members per WUA. To ensure the long-term
sustainability of WUAs, mechanisms for sanctions against members in case of non-compliance with
membership duties, and provisions for conflict resolution, would be established, as set out the in draft
WUA Legislation prepared under the Critical Imports Project.
14.   Preparatory Phase.  Establishment of WUAs under the project would involve local WUA
promoters within district-based WUA Units, assisted by an expatriate expert. During a four-months
preparatory phase (January to April 1994), financed under a Project Preparation Facility, district-based
WUA Units would be set up and start working on establishing WUAs in the project area. Assisted by
expatriate experts, WUA promoters in the Units will motivate farmers to join into WUAs. The Project
Management Unit (PMU) would monitor the progress during the preparatory and the following phases of
WUAs establishment, and the expatriate WUA expert and the leaders of district-based WUA Units would
report to PMU staff. At the end of the preparatory phase (May 1994), the expatriate WUA trainer, the
PMU Director and Bank staff would review the results.
15.   Project Implementation. Following the preparatory phase, the work of the WUA Units would
concentrate on: resolving initial problems related to the legal and organizational creation of WUAs; long-
term counseling for, and conflict resolution between WUA members; and, starting in 1996, promoting the
gradual consolidation and grouping of village-based WUA into larger water users groups that are more
closely representing hydrological units. Two expatriate experts would provide continuous assistance to
WUA Units in the project area until end-1994 (total of 18 man/months in 1994). Assistance from
expatriate experts would be reduced to 9 man/months during 1995 and 3 man/mans per year between 1996
and 1998. Between 1994 and 1996, an average of 12 local full-time WUA promoters would be required
in each WUA Unit, reduced to 5 promoters per Unit in subsequent years. The number of WUA Units
would be gradually decreased, starting in the fourth year of project implementation, to half of the initial
number by the end of the project implementation period.
16.   Risks for Successful WIA Establishment. Lack of farmer motivation, caused by a general
reservation to any form of collective organization, would be a potential project risk. Strict adherence to
democratic principles in the formation of WUAs, with maximum farmer participation in the design of the
structure and in the determination of the rules and functions of WUAs, would minimize this risk.
Additional incentives through the limitation of rehabilitation works to areas where WUAs have been
successfully established, and through the allocation of irrigation water to members of WUAs only, in
accordance with the new Irrigation Code, would further promote farmer interest in WUAs.

ANNEX 5.6: On-Farm Water Management Program
1.    Background. The dissolution of state farms and agricultural cooperatives has dramatically changed
land tenure and farming conditions in Albania's large irrigation schemes. A multitude of small farms with
average size of 1.4 ha have emerged in the project area. The new private holdings are operated by largely
unexperienced farmers using family hand labor. Cropping patterns are in part still oriented towards
producing cereals and forage crops. Water distribution to farmers is erratic since neither irrigation systems
were designed, nor WEs are equipped, to serve a smallholder farmer situation. Rehabilitation of irrigation
and drainage infrastructure supported by the project will substantially imnprove the reliability and equity of
water deliveries to the farm gate. However, the project will have a modest impact only on the actual water
quantities delivered to farmers which result from the low parameters accepted for the original system
design. A significant increase of water deliveries would require an expensive upgrading of entire systems,
including reservoirs and related distribution infrastructure, which would not be justified from an economic
point of view under the current constraints to distribution and marketing of agricultural output.
2.     Rationale for On-Farm Water Management Improvement. The water scarcity resulting from
system under-design is likely to be exacerbated by increasing water demand from farmers as their economic
survival will require the shift from the current low value field crops to highly remunerative vegetables and
fruits grown under intensive cropping patterns. In this context, increasing the productivity of limited water
deliveries at the farm level is of a high significance for the sustainability of irrigated agriculture.
Furthermore, the increase of water charges to cost-covering levels would be a powerful incentive for the
rational use of water by farmers. Increasing water productivity would be made possible through a
combination of three objectives including (i) reducing water losses through improved on-farm irrigation
facilities and techniques; (ii) accurately meeting actual crop water requirements through adequate irrigation
scheduling; and (iii) enhancing the biological transformation of water into vegetal matter through efficient
farming practices. The project would focus on the first two objectives which are related to on-farm water
management.
3.     Proposed On-Farm  Water Management Program.  A reduction of water losses would be
obtained from (i) lining of farm ditches (quaternary canals), (ii) precise land levelling of irrigated fields,
and (iii) use of adequate irrigation methods. Adequate scheduling of water applications by farmers would
result from a real-time estimate of plant water needs on a local experimentation center and the timely
dissemination of this information to farmers.
4.     Project Approach. The project would make a first step in the improvement of on-farm water
management by focussing on the acquisition of the required scientific equipment, organizing field
demonstrations on farms selected on a voluntary basis, and disseminating basic information on water
requirements. Farmers would also be informed and trained about the safe application of agro-chemicals,
based on information brochures in local language developed under financing of the FY92 Critical Imports
Project. The program would be limited to two districts (Tirana and Lushnje) and be implemented by the
Soil Institute (SI) and the Extension Service (ES) of MoAF, under the overall coordination by the Institute
for Study and Design of Water Works (ISDWW) in Tirana.
5.     SI would be responsible for an experimentation program to be implemented on its Tirana and
Lushnje field stations, including:



- 93 -                                          Annex 5
Page 23 of 23
(a) testing inexpensive methods for field ditch lining and land levelling;
(b) testing adequate furrow and border irrigation techniques;
(c) testing modern irrigation methods (drip irrigation, microjets, etc.); and
(d) measuring evapo-transpiration and estimating crop consumptive use and water requirements.
In addition, SI would carry out the following tasks in the all districts of the project area:
(e) estimating the parameters for surface irrigation on the demonstration farms; and
(f) measuring water and soil salinity in the areas exposed to salinization.
6.     The Extension Service would be responsible for:
(a) organizing demonstrations of improved irrigation practices on selected pilot farms in the Tirana and
Lushnje districts, using the parameters determined by SI; and
(b) disseminating information on real-time water requirements to farmers on a pilot basis in the all
project districts.
7. Resources Required for Implementation. The project would finance the acquisition of required
laboratory, scientific and office equipment and vehicles for SI and ISDWW. Scientific equipment would
include two climatological stations, lysimeters, tensiometers, and demonstration sets for drip irrigation and
microjets (0.5 ha each). Furthermore, technical assistance would be financed under the project in the areas
of bioclimatology, surface irrigation and soil salinity. Two training sessions would be organized to train
local trainers from the Extension Service and 15 training sessions would subsequently be organized with
farmers.
8.    Implementation Arrangements. Implementing of the described program lies within the scope of
SI's and ES's regular activities for which required staff and facilities are available. To coordinate the
program, a Water Management Unit would be created within ISDWW and would initially include one
specialist. The terms of reference of the Unit would also cover the coordination and evaluation of off-farm
water management activities by Water Enterprises in the project area, including, in particular, monitoring
of the efficiency of water distribution and an assessment of the environmental impact of irrigation and
drainage.

ALBANIA
IRRIGATION REHABILITATION PROJECT
FINANCIAL AND ECONOMIC ANALYSIS
ANNEX 8.1: Financial Analysis
Agricultural Production
1.    Crop Yields. The project would increase the amount of irrigation water available at the crop by
an average of 400 m3/ha, in order to reach a total of 1,600 m3/ha, for an aggregate command area of
45,019 ha of at present under-irrigated land. In addition, 28,467 ha of currently rainfed areas would return
to irrigation at 1,600 m3/ha following rehabilitation works under the project. The project would also
improve drainage on a total area of 99,304 ha. Improved irrigation and drainage would result in higher
crop yields. However, due to the poor current cash position of farmers, use of fertilizer and pesticides will
remain limited over a number of years. Therefore, crop yields are expected to remain below maximum
achievable levels, as shown in figure 1, for major crops in the project area, until all required farm inputs
would be used in appropriate quantities by the fifth year of project implementation. It is assumed that
fertilizer and pesticide use would equal 20 percent of full requirements in year 1, increasing to 100 percent
by year 5.

2.    Cropping Intensity and Patterns. Improved irrigation would also allow for higher cropping
intensities through introduction of double cropping, causing an expected increase of present average
cropping intensities of 90 percent in the project area to about 150 percent. Furthermore, farmers are
expected to change their cropping patterns due to improved irrigation and drainage, in order to include
more cash crops and other crops requiring irrigation such as maize, summer forage and irrigated wheat.
Figure 2 shows the assumed change in cropping patterns for areas that are currently rainfed which would

3.     Incremental Production.  Without any marketing constraints, the project would result in an
estimated incremental agricultural production, expressed as output per year over a twenty year period, as
follows: 12,000 tons of wheat, equivalent to an 18 percent rise in production when compared to a base
case without the project; 24,000 tons of maize (113 percent rise); 450 tons of fodder (70 percent rise);
9,200 tons of tomatoes (173 percent rise); 2,400 tons of potatoes (117 percent rise); 19,700 tons of other
vegetables (51 percent rise); 23.5 million liters of milk (27 percent rise); 500 tons of tobacco (127 percent
rise); and 3,200 tons of cotton (128 percent rise).  However, marketing of produce is at present
significantly constrained due to consumers' low domestic purchasing power, lacking marketing and



- 109 -                                    Annex 8
Page 3 of 19
distribution infrastructure, and limnited access to export markets. Therefore, in the financial and economic
analysis of the project, assumptions are made such as to reduce the amount of produced and marketed
output during the first three years of project implementation (see below). Predictions for a potential shift
in cropping patterns due to restricted marketing options can only be made with high uncertainty and have
been excluded from the analysis.

Farm Income
4.    Assumptions for Farm Models. The seven districts in the project area can be divided into two
agro-climatic zones (see Annex 1.3): The Central Coastal Plains (Zone 1), including the districts of Durres,
Kavaja, Lushnje and Fier, and the Northern Coastal Area (Zone 2) with the districts of Laci, Kruje and
Tirana. For each of the two agro-clirnatic zones, two farm production models have been developed,
reflecting a shift from either rainfed or under-irrigated conditions to an improved irrigation level of 1,600
m3/ha (see Attachment 1 for farm models in Zone 1). The average farm size for Zone 1 equals 1.41 ha
and for Zone 2 1.08 ha. Most incremental production would initially be for self-consumption, with the
assumed share of marketed surplus production rising from 25 percent in year one of the project to 100
percent by year four. Current local price levels of farm inputs and outputs are assumed to reach world
market levels over a five-year period (see Attachment 2). Farmers are expected to use mostly family labor
to cultivate and harvest crops, but they would continue to hire outside mechanization services for plowing
and tilling. It is assumed that credit for inputs and system repairs would be available, at a real interest rate
of 8 percent per year, to finance 25 percent of operating costs and on-farm investments. Water charges
are not incorporated into farm models in order to receive net farm cash flows as a basis for determining
farmers' ability to pay water charges out of annual net cash income.
5.    Farm Production Values. Under the above assumptions, the farm models reveal that per-ha
production values of tomatoes and other high-value vegetables would increase significantly from their
present low levels of about US$ 1,000 per ha and year to more than US$ 8,000 per ha and year by year
five of the project (see figure 3). Accordingly, the share of vegetables (incl. tomatoes and potatoes) among
annual farm production values would rise from about 10 percent in 1994 to 30 percent by 1998 (figure 4).

6.    Expected Farm Cash-Flow. Under the project, net farm cash-flows, after on-farm consumption,
would remain negative during the first two years of the project, caused by the assumed initial constraints
for marketing of surplus production. Farmers' average annual net cash income would rise above US$ 100
equivalent by year three and would exceed US$ 500 equivalent by year five (see figure 5). This compares
favorably to farm incomes without the project of no more than US$ 200 equivalent by year five. Due to
the phasing of works, only some 2,300 farm families in the project area would be expected to start
benefitting from investinents under the project in the second year of imnplementation, rising to some 56,800
families at the end of project implementation, or 45 percent of the total of 126,000 families in the project
area. Additional beneficiaries of the project would include providers of farm services and any incremental
hired farm labor.

ANNEX 8.2: Cost Recovery
7.    Cost of System O&M. In the absence of any reliable accounting data at the WE level, costs for
system operation and maintenance (O&M) have been estimated for each of the seven project districts.
O&M costs have been split into a fixed component, applicable to the total command area in each district,
rising from US$ 10 to US$ 20 per ha over the project period; and a variable component, applicable to the
actual operating area, rising from US$ 10 to up to US$ 40 per ha by 1999, depending on the amount of
pumping involved and electricity required in each district. As a result, current total costs for system O&M
of primary and secondary canals by WEs are estimated to range between US$ 24 and 43 per ha, with an
average cost of US$ 32 per ha (see Attachment 3). Over the implementation period of the project, these
costs are estimated to rise to an average of US$ 51 per ha by year 6 of the project, reflecting mainly the
expected increase in labor costs and energy prices (current electricity prices of US$ 0.018/kWh are
assumed to rise to about US$ 0.05/kWh to reach cost-covering levels). The cost of operation and
maintenance of systems below the secondary level by farmers is estimated to equal no more than US$ 10
per year and ha and would be recovered through WUA fees.
8.    Cost of Investments.  The total cost of investments under the project, including design and
engineering fees and required subsequent replacement investments, translate into a per-ha charge of about
US$ 15 per year, assuming that farmers in the project area would be granted a five-year grace period with
subsequent repayments over 35 years, reflecting the conditions of the IDA Credit.  Any on-farm
investments into the infrastructure below the secondary canal level would be financed by individual farmers
or WUAs.
9.    Cost Recovery Policy. As a result of the above, under full cost recovery, required annual revenues
from water charges, payable by farmers to WEs in the project area, would need to average US$ 32 per
ha during 1994, rising to about US$ 66 per ha by 1999. However, net cash-flows for private smallholders
in the project area, after on-farm consumption of outputs, would only become positive by year three of the
project (see para 6). Assuming a required annual minimum cash income of US$ 100 equivalent per farmer,
farmers would only be able to pay fully cost-covering water charges, and additional WUA fees, by year
five of the project. Therefore, a cost recovery policy would be adopted providing for the gradual increase
of water charges to cost-covering levels over a five-year period. Given the currently irrigated area, and
assuming a collection rate of 60 percent of billed water charges, the present charge level of Lek 1.8/m3
translates into an annual average revenue of US$ 13 equivalent per ha (see Attachment 3). This represents
a coverage level of 41 percent of the current estimated costs for system operation and maintenance of US$
32 equivalent per ha. Under the project, this percentage would be increased by 15 percent per year,
through annual revisions of water charges, to achieve the full coverage of operation and maintenance costs
borne by WEs in the project area by year five of the project, i.e, by 1998. In addition, starting in 1999,
water charges would be increased further to reflect the full recovery of Government-financed project
investments and required replacement costs.
10.   Water Charges. Under the above assumptions for per-ha costs borne by WEs and investment
costs, and assuming a gradual rise in the collection rate for water charges to 80 percent by year five of the
project, cost-covering water charges would need to rise from their present level of Lek 1.8/i3 to an
average of Lek 4.8/m3 by 1999. Water charges in 1999 would range from Lek 3.3/m3 in the Laci district
to Lek 5.2/m3 in the district of Durres. This takes into account the conservative assumption made under
the project of a comparatively low consumption rate after system rehabilitation (1,600 m3/ha/year, see table
5.3). Assuming that a higher average consumption rate (2,500 m3/ha/year) would be achievable, water
charges would need to rise to no more than an average price of Lek 3. 1/m3 to be fully cost-covering. An
increase in the collection rate for water charges would further reduce the required rise in the price of
irrigation water, to an average charge level of Lek 2.3/m3 by 1999 under an assumed collection rate of 100

Page 6 of 19
percent (see table 1).

ANNEX 8.3: Economic Analysis
12.          Basic Assumptions. Over a twenty year period, both incremental recurrent and replacement
costs have been added to investment costs under the project to reflect the full economic costs of the project.
In accordance with the project implementation schedule, phasing of construction works has been assumed
such that 4 percent of the works would be completed in 1995, followed by cumulative completion rates of
27, 55, 80 and 100 percent for years 1996-1999. Incremental farm production was estimated assuming
that each farm in the project area would achieve maximum yields and incremental outputs with a five-year
time-lag after completion of rehabilitation works in order to allow for a temporarily limited use of fertilizer
and pesticides. Current marketing constraints for agricultural produce have been taken into consideration
by reducing the economic value of farm production by 75, 50 and 25 percent during the first three years
of project implementation since farmers are expected to decrease production of non-marketable surplus
output, after satisfying their on-farm consumption needs, if a change in cropping patterns would not resolve
the marketing constraint.
13.          Economic Prices. Since no standard conversion factor (SCF) for Albania exists, economic
prices have been calculated, using different methods depending on the nature of the input or output, as
follows (see the table on economic prices in Attachment 4):
(a) Tradeable outputs such as wheat and maize, which are net imports, and tobacco and cotton, which
are net exports are valued at corresponding import or export parity prices at the farm-gate.
Tradeable project inputs such as fertilizer are valued in the same manner;
(b) Locally consumed outputs and locally produced inputs, such as crops grown as animal feed and
crop seeds, are valued at prevailing local financial prices;
(c) Unskilled labor for agricultural production is valued at its opportunity cost which is assumed to be
close to zero since there are almost no alternative employment possibilities for rural farm labor.
A shadow wage rate of Lek 50/day (US$ 0.50 equivalent) is assumed, rising to Lek 360/day within
ten years after project start, thus assuming that employment opportunities in the rural area will
improve;
(d) Unskilled labor for project construction works is valued at its opportunity cost, assuming that
currently about 50 percent of the unskilled labor force would be unable find any employment, while
the remainder would find employment at the official current Government minimum wage rate of
Lek 45/hour (Lek 360/day). Indirect costs, amounting to 2.36 times the direct labor cost, are not
factored into the economic wage rate. These assumptions result in a shadow wage rate of Lek
180/day for the five-year project construction phase;
(e) Skilled labor for project construction works is priced at the current financial labor market wage rate
of Lek 71/hour, reflecting the large and growing demand for construction works (roads, buildings,
other public infrastructure) in Albania. Consequently, all skilled workers are assumed to be able
to find similar employments as a next best alternative;
(f) Transportation and agricultural mechanization costs are valued at current financial market costs,
rising to international averages within five years. The rise to international prices takes into account
equipment depreciation, repairs and maintenance, and replacement costs for obsolete farm
equipment. The average assumed equivalent world market price for farm mechanization equipment
(including fuel, labor to run the equipment, depreciation, repairs and maintenance) is US$ 20 per
hour;
(g) Water charges for irrigation are assumed at US$ 50 per ha for system operation and maintenance.
Infrastructure investment costs have been included in the project's total investment costs.

14.   ERR and Sensitivity Analysis. The weighted average economic rate of return for the project, taking
into account all project components, equals 17 percent. ERRS for individual project districts are shown in
figure 6, assuming an allocation of project costs that are not linked to specific districts in proportion to
district-based system rehabilitation costs (see table in Attachment 5). Switching values have been calculated
assuming a benchmark discount rate of 12 percent. The results reveal that project costs would have to rise
by 35 percent, or project benefits drop by 25 percent compared to appraisal values to make project
investments non-viable (see figure 7). An increase in construction costs of more than 35 percent is highly
unlikely given the advanced stage of project design. A reduction of benefits by more than 25 percent is
equally unlikely in light of the already conservative assumptions made about farmers' ability to market
incremental surplus production. The project would also remain economically viable under the assumption
of a two-year time-lag for expected benefits to materialize.
FIGURE 7: Sensitvity Analysis
